Page i of vi 
Abstract 
High-rises sprout all over the country, but the knowledge regarding the necessary fire safety measures, fire safety 
guidelines concerning building taller than 45 meters and knowledge regarding intervention in high-rises lag 
behind in Denmark.  
Therefore, is it chosen to investigate if the Fire Brigade Intervention Model (FBIM) can be of use in Denmark, if 
FBIM can be used to identify effects of different intervention strategies, and what impact using stairs vs. a 
firefighter lift has on the intervention.  
A series of interviews have been conducted with different Danish and foreign Fire and Rescue services in order 
to identify the different intervention strategies used in the case of fire in a high-rise. Fire and rescues services in 
in Denmark as well as Sweden, Finland and the US have been interviewed. The different discovered intervention 
strategies have been analyzed in FBIM and the result have been used in a comparative analysis. The different 
identified intervention strategies have been applied on a specific building case in order to form a basis for 
comparison.  
The result shows a positive effect on intervention times when a carefully planned standard operation procedure 
(SOP) is put in to use. A difference in total intervention time, time for reaching the fire compartment ready to 
conduct search and rescue and time of when additional resources will arrive are identified when the different 
intervention strategies are compared. The result also shows the effect employment of firefighters, which have a 
negative effect on intervention time. If stairs are used instead of lift, the time taken to reach the fire 
compartment is increased by a factor of 3.0 - 3.4 depending on employment of the firefighters.  
 
Resumé 
Højhusbyggeriet skyder op over hele landet, men viden om de nødvendige brandsikkerheds foranstaltninger, 
retningslinjer for brandsikkerhed i bygninger højere end 45 meter og viden om brandvæsnets indsats procedure 
i højhusene halter bagefter i Danmark. 
Derfor er det valgt at undersøge, om den Australske model ”the Fire Brigade Intervention Model” (FBIM) kan 
anvendes i en dansk kontekst, om FBIM kan identificere effekten ved brugen af forskellige indsats procedure, og 
hvilken effekt brugen af trapper kontra brandmandselevator har på hele indsatsen tiden. 
Der er blevet gennemført en række interviews med forskellige danske og udenlandske beredskaber. Dette med 
henblik på at identificere forskellige standard indsats procedurer i tilfælde af brand i et højhus. Beredskaber fra 
Danmark samt Sverige, Finland og USA er blevet interviewet. De forskellige identificerede indsats procedure er 
analyseret i FBIM, og resultaterne underlagt en komparativ analyse. De forskellige identificerede indsats 
procedure er blevet anvendt på et bestemt højhus projekt for at danne grundlag for en sammenligning af 
resultater. 
Resultaterne viser en positiv effekt på indsatstiden ved brug af en nøje planlagt indsats strategi ned skrevet i en 
i standard operation procedure (SOP). En forskel i den samlede indsatstid, tiden til at røgdykkerholdet er klar til 
indsats på etagen, og tidspunktet for, hvornår yderligere ressourcer vil ankomme, kan identificeres, når de 
forskellige indsats procedure sammenlignes. Resultaterne viser også en positiv effekt af brug af 
brandmandselevator kontra brug af trapper. Hvis trapper anvendes i stedet for elevator, øges tiden for hvornår 
røgdykkerne er klar på etagen med faktor 3,0 – 3,4 afhængigt af om der er tale om fuldtids eller deltids 
beredskaber.

Johanne Salten – S170331 
 
Page ii of vi 
Preface 
This master thesis is written by Johanne Salten as partial fulfillment of Mater in Fire Safety at Technical University 
of Denmark. The Master’s thesis performs a study regarding the Fire Brigade Interventions Model (FBIM) in 
relation to intervention strategies in high-rise buildings. 
 
The Master’s thesis is written for staff members in a Fire and Rescue Service, as well as staff members in an 
engineering consultant company working within the fields of fire safety. It is expected that the reader has some 
basic background knowledge about fire safety engineering and the Fire and Rescue Service in general. 
 
The Master’s thesis is heavy on the images, and the author is aware of this. But it is chosen to complete the 
Master’s thesis with these as they are beneficial for the understanding of FBIM. Images that  support the Master’s 
thesis, but that are not essential in relation to the main goal, are to be found in the appendix. 
 
Regarding the title of the different intervention participants, it is chosen to use the Danish titles such as 
indsatsleder (ISL) and holdleder (HL) when referring to Danish intervention procedures, and more general titles 
such as on-site-incident commander and captain in relation to international intervention procedures. 
Indsatsleder corresponds to on-site-incident commander and holdleder corresponds to captain. The titles for 
these positions differ from country to country, and as the focus in this Master’s thesis is FBIM in a Danish context, 
it is chosen to use Danish titles. Translation of different written standard operation is a free translation donned 
by the author.  
 
A variety of methods will be used in the master thesis. These are described in relevant sections throughout the 
Master’s thesis and in continuation of the problem statement. A critical review of different methods has been 
conducted and is a part of the discussion. 
 
The author has a work history within the Fire and Rescue Service as a firefighter and later as holdleder (HL). At 
the time of writing, the author works as a fire safety consultant in a private company. This work history gives the 
author a pre-understanding of the relationship between different parties in this field of work. 
 
This Master’s thesis is not a validation of the FBIM program and the data within it. It is assumed data within the 
program is valid. The author is aware of the differences between intervention methods in Australian and in 
Denmark, but since the scope is to define whether FBIM can be used in a Danish context and can analyze the 
difference between intervention strategies , the data within the program is adequate for now. If FBIM is to be 
incorporated in Fire and Rescue Services and in other fields, a more exhaustive study of drills and data within 
FBIM should be conducted. Calculations done in this Master’s thesis include charts1 1 through 12. These charts 
identify the initial intervention. The calculation does not include a fire model, etc., and therefore there is no 
analysis of suppression time. The Master’s thesis does not cover the fire safety issues when the building is under 
construction. This subject is just as important, especially in relation to high-rise buildings. 
Data results found in this Master’s thesis are not to be used in connection with specific project design or 
engineering. The data used in this Master’s thesis are based on the current data from Australia, and it cannot 
directly be transposed and used without further research in Denmark. The data in this Master’s thesis cannot be 
used in a comparative analysis in connection with specific project design or engineering of buildings. 
 
 
 
                                                                
 
1 Term ”chart” and “flowchart” will be used as synonym though out this Master’s thesis

Page 1 of 82 
1 Introduction 
This section describes the context of why the chosen topic is relevant, the problem statement and which methods 
are used to investigate and answer the problems. The overall composition of the Master’s thesis is also described. 
1.1 Background  
Thomas Schleidt writes in an op-ed in the newspaper and media “Ingeniøren” 2: 
 
“…High-rises sprout, but the knowledge about fire safety in these types of buildings 
have not kept pace with it. This is unfortunate, when there is no acknowledged 
guideline for buildings higher than 45 meters…. 
 
…We also believe that there is a lack of an overall method of verifying if the safety 
level in tall buildings actually meets the requirement set out in the building code...” 
(ing.dk,2017.04.20) 
 
High-rise buildings are mesmerizing in many ways. In relation to fire and safety measures, the height can have a 
negative effect if not accounted for. Evacuation, the rescue of persons, basic intervention strategies and 
intervention takes more time and becomes complex compared with low-rise buildings, even when the 
intervention procedure is more or less the same. Accessways are limited to only from the inside of the building 
via stairs or elevators, which most likely is also the only evacuation route. The stairs in combination with lifts 
become essential. The risk of getting trapped above the fire is present as the firefighters are no longer able to 
access the building from the outside with a ladder appliance. Creating an overview of the incident and locating 
the fire becomes difficult and more critical due to the height. Securing water supply in the upper floors becomes 
a challenge and great detail must be paid to this in the design phase as establishing and securing alternative 
water supply measures in the upper floors are limited. Basic intervention strategies, such as firefighters venting 
a room to increase visibility, become a challenge as windows are not opening, or, if they are, wind-driven fires 
become an issue [1] [2]. 
 
Lack of access options and possibilities to exhaust smoke from a room by a window are just some of the many 
deviations in the fire and rescue service (FRS)3 intervention methods, which occurs when intervention in buildings 
below and above twenty-two meters are compared. Twenty-two meters refer to the height from ground floor to 
the top floor in the building. Buildings above twenty-two meters are, in this Master’s thesis, defined as a high-
rise. 
The most challenging deviation is in the reconnaissance of the situation conducted by the on-site incident 
commander (OIC). This is normally done from the ground and with “a trip around the building,”. This is not 
possible in the case of a high-rise fire. In this situation, it is most likely impossible to see anything from the ground. 
The overview must be a combination of inside observation reports and information from technical systems [2]. 
As pointed out by Rasmus Storegaard from Beredskab Øst in the before mentioned op-ed, the lack of standards 
for the dry riser, fire alarm panels and other relevant elements in relation to intervention is problematic. This 
means the decision of what is adequate is laid out to the individual FRS. This can be difficult as all buildings must 
be more extravagant than the last one and knowledge in this field is not gathered. 
The above in relation to the lack of a proper method for verification if the safety level in tall buildings meets the 
building code requirements is a poor combination. This is where the Fire Brigade Intervention Model (FBIM) can 
become a relevant and important tool. Modern fire engineering design tools, such as fire and evacuation 
                                                                
 
2 The op-ed is in Danish. The above text is a free translation by this author  
3 The terms “Fire and Rescue service”, “Fire brigade” and “Fire department” are synonymous throughout this document.  
==PAGE:12==
 
 
 
Johanne Salten – S170331 
 
Page 2 of 82 
modeling and performance-based design regulations, gives the opportunity to custom design a building. This can 
result in altering some buildings elements which firefighters would expect to find within a certain building type. 
This could be the time of fire resistance of construction elements, special design ventilation systems, and 
flammable surfaces. Without an analysis of the impact of such deviations upon firefighters and the FRS 
intervention, their safety and ability to complete their duties may have been compromised. 
 
FBIM is a tool for fire safety consultants as well as the FRS. FBIM provides a methodology that can quantify the 
intervention by the FRS so the functional role of the FRS can be incorporated into the building design [3]. In 
relation to the FRS, FBIM can be used in many different aspects, as pointed out in an interview with René 
Ruusunen: 
 
“…A Danish Fire Brigade Intervention Model (FBIM) could be an essential 
foundation for the risk-based dimensioning [1] of the municipal fire and rescue 
services. Regarding a service level agreement, FBIM would bring more to the 
equation, than the turnout time for the fire and rescue service. FBIM could offer a 
way to quantify the fire and rescue services operations. It could provide more 
standardized information on the resources and time required to carry out the 
needed fire intervention in specific scenarios, e.g., a high-rise fire. Furthermore, a 
Danish FBIM could provide the fire and rescue service with a benchmark to which 
they continuously must comply and therefore be used to justify their various 
training and preparation. It would support the ongoing process for the fire and 
rescue service to excel when performing their duties. 
 
FBIM, if used in correlation with the risk assessment, could provide common terms 
and help building owners, the municipality and consultants with a fundamental risk 
insight and understanding…” (Ruusunen 2019, Personal interview) 
 
 
Performance-based fire design consists of two to three elements today. It is often based upon a fire model, e.g. 
a CFD simulation of the fire and an evacuation model. The results are compared, and the fire safety level is 
determined. In some cases, the combined analysis is accompanied by a risk assessment. In addition to this, a 
short description of the possibilities of intervention by the FRS is provided. 
This two-component process could, with respect to the FRS and the building regulation 2018 (BR18) §82 section 
2 point 5, be expanded to include a more careful analysis of the possibilities of an intervention by the FRS. This 
is also with respect to the safety of the FRS. They put their lives at risk to save others. 
FBIM can connect the fire model with the intervention of the FRS, as it determines the time from notification to 
when water is first applied, see Figure 1-1. This time frame is of importance, as it makes a difference when water 
is applied in relation to the magnitude of the damage the fire can cost. FBIM could perhaps be used to determine 
the most efficient intervention method in relation to a certain building. 
 
==PAGE:13==
 
 
 
Johanne Salten – S170331 
 
Page 3 of 82 
 
Figure 1-1: FBIM in relation to fire model and evacuation model. Green is evacuation, blue is critical time in relation to occupants and red is 
intervention.    
In 2012, Lise Kamstrup and Therese Franck conducted a series of full-scale field tests on intervention time steps 
in high-rise buildings [1]. In 2014, Jan Uldahl Riis, Peter Dræbye and Rasmus Storgaard Petersen conducted a 
series of exercise tests on a variety of common intervention procedures [4]. The result of the study may serve as 
input for FBIM or an indicator of validation. 
 
1.2 Problem statement 
The increased use of performance-based methods to design high-rise buildings requires a need for a more 
informed understanding of fire brigade intervention strategies and times. It is relevant as it provides the needed 
documentation in relation to BR18, if it is ensured, the FRS have the options for searching for and rescuing 
persons and options for carrying out the extinguishing measures which are necessary in order to search for and 
rescue persons and limit the spreading of the fire. It becomes more relevant when a building is tall and/or 
complex as time is of the essence. FBIM could provide a way of quantifying the FRS intervention in relation to 
the building design. Therefore, it is chosen to conduct an analysis of whether FBIM can be used in a Danish 
context and if it can be used to identify the effects of different intervention methods via comparative analysis. 
t9 
==PAGE:14==
 
 
 
Johanne Salten – S170331 
 
Page 4 of 82 
In relation to intervention methods, it is chosen to conduct an analysis of foreign FRS procedures as well as 
Danish, as high-rise buildings are still not a common building type in Denmark. 
Below, the research questions that are to be answered in this Master’s thesis are listed: 
 
 
What is the effect of the different Danish types of standard interventions methods in high-rise 
buildings? 
 
What is the effect of the different international types of standard interventions methods in high-rise 
buildings? 
 
What effect does a firefighter lift have vs. a regular stair? 
 
Does the time for firefighters to reach the fire compartment ready for search and rescue differ in 
relation to different interventions strategies and number of resources? 
 
What are the conditions for using FBIM in a Danish context? 
 
The questions above will be answered by using different methods. 
Data gathering regarding Danish and international intervention strategies will be collected via a qualitative 
investigation. A series of interviews will be conducted in order to gather relevant information regarding 
intervention strategies and understanding of the FRS. Available descriptions of interventions strategies, also 
known as standard operations procedures (SOP), will be gathered and examined. The different FRS intervention 
strategies are not public domain, and it is not common practice in Denmark to have written manuals as seen in 
other foreign FRS. Variations in interventions strategies are expected due to self-rule in the different Danish FRS; 
therefore, it is necessary to conduct a series of interviews. On the basis of the gathered information via interviews 
and SOPs, a set of interventions strategies will be described with a view to be used in FBIM. The analysis of the 
different intervention strategies and calculation of the different times of the intervention will be done in FBIM 
by hand. A comparative analysis of the results will be performed to identify the different effects and establish if 
FBIM can be used in a Danish context. The different methods used are described in more detail in the relevant 
sections throughout the Master’s thesis. 
 
1.3 Composition 
This Master’s thesis consists of different overall sections. Section 1 servers as an introduction of the scope of the 
Master’s thesis. Section 2.1 provides a historical background knowledge regarding the Danish Fire Brigade and 
the overall organization of it. This section important as it forms a basis for the assumptions made later on in the 
Master’s thesis. Sections 2.2 and 2.2 provide the results of the qualitative investigation of the different 
interventions procedures, hence a description of the different identified intervention procedures, which are later 
used in the quantitative analysis in FBIM. Section 3 describes FBIM, the idea behind it and how it is used in 
Australia currently. Section 3.2 describes the calculation procedure in FBIM, how to apply the method and the 
different elements within FBIM. Section 4 provides all the necessary information regarding the case, 
measurement, and basis of the calculations. Calculation results and discussions of these are found in section 5. 
The conclusion is found in section 6.  
 
==PAGE:15==
 
 
 
Johanne Salten – S170331 
 
Page 5 of 82 
2 Theory 
Section 2 describes different information and background knowledge for this Master’s thesis. A description of 
the organization of the Danish Fire and Rescue service is given as well as information regarding national and 
international intervention procedures. 
2.1 Background knowledge regarding the Danish Fire and Rescues service 
Section 2.1 describes how the Danish FRS is organized nationally and the historical background for this. This is of 
importance as there is a difference between the FRS’s across the country, which is conveyed in the result of the 
conducted interviews shown later in section 2.2. 
 
2.1.1 Fire and Rescue Services Organization Today 
The Danish FRS today consists of two levels, one on the municipal level and one on the national level. Each level 
has different responsibilities in relation to each other, but the overall goal is the same. The goal is to prevent, 
reduce and remedy any damage or injury inflicted on human life, property or the environment as a consequence 
of accidents or disasters [5] [6]. 
 
Level 1 is the municipal FRS4. The municipality must have an FRS but can choose to have it as a municipal FRS or 
hire a private company to fulfill the task. Whichever form is chosen, the FRS can be either a full time, part-time, 
a volunteer or a combination of the three. When a fire or natural disaster occurs, the first turn-out must depart 
from a municipal FRS, unless it is under specific circumstances which are out of the scope of the report, within a 
defined time and with the necessary resources. The time is determined in an agreement between the FRS and 
the city council5. The time cannot exceed the time frame set out in the consolidation act of risk-based fire and 
rescue service6 (Act nr 765 of 03/08/2005) which is set to five minutes acc. to §7. It is of interest to notice that, 
in Denmark, departure time is defined and not time of arrival. In many countries, the arrival time is set out 
instead. This makes more sense in relation to FBIM since it has an impact on the success rate of extinguishing a 
fire when the FRS arrive and are ready. In many FRS, there is also a defined limit for time of arrival, this is agreed 
upon with the local city council. This is often with a with reference to the time frame used before 2005. 
 
The technical intervention of the FRS at the scene is managed by the municipal Indsatsleder (ISL/OIC) in 
teamwork with the holdleder (HL/CAPT.) and the firefighters, the police, etc. The overall response is coordinated 
by the police [5]. 
 
Today (2018), there are 24 municipal FRS, most of them are organized as what is called a §60-corporation7. Figure 
2-1 shows the coverage areas for each FRS with a color. Each of the individual municipalities within the FRS are 
shown with a light gray line. The location of the individual fire station is not shown. Before 2016.01.01, most 
municipalities had independent FRS [7]. At the moment, some corporations are undergoing restructuring 
changes, and there can therefore in the future be more than 24 municipal FRS.

5 Beredskabskommisionen

Level 2 is the national FRS8. The municipal FRS can request special 
equipment, large amounts of equipment and/or personnel in the 
case of comprehensive accidents. This can be serviceable when an 
accident takes time (days) and/or require lots of manpower. The 
municipal FRS must, in any case, sustain a reasonable safety level 
in the rest of the coverage area, all resources cannot be allocated 
to one incident. The national FRS is in five different locations in 
Denmark and is governed by the state. Figure 2-2 shows the 
location of the five national FES, the different municipal FRS and 
the types of the different incidents these handles. Both the 
municipal and national FRS work does not only cover fire incidents, 
but also incidents with chemicals, storm surge, oil pollution, traffic 
incident, contamination of drinking water, bomb incidents, etc. In 
some cases, the FRS manage the incident on their own, but in most 
cases, the FRS work together with other responsible authorities 
such as police, health authorities, etc.

The training level and education of all firefighters, HL and ISL have the same fundamental basis, but over time in 
the field, working firefighters, HL and ISL acquire local knowledge and training exercises that are adjusted to local 
needs. This can vary throughout the whole organization [8] [9]. 
 
The necessary equipment and FRS organization (level of service) are, in each FRS, based upon a risk-based 
assessment conducted by the FRS and agreed upon with the city council. The level of service is based on the risk 
assessment. The level of service can change over the years, and it is of great impotence that all risk elements are 
featured in the assessment [6]. No fire safety aspect may be omitted due to no fire or incident in an element 
over the years. Fire safety aspects may only be omitted if they no longer exist in the coverage area of the FRS or 
it is shown to be handled in another way. The risk-based approach was enforced in 2005. 
 
The assessment is done every election period within the city council. The budget for the FRS is distributed 
accordingly. New and extravagant building types and designs within an FRS coverage area have an impact on the 
budget. If no buildings in the coverage area are alike, it is likely that the FRS do not have the knowledge, 
resources, equipment and/or manpower to deal with an incident in that building at first. There is no doubt about 
it that the necessary resources are available in the FRS in Denmark as a whole since the FRS can call another level 
1 service or a level 2 service. But when fighting fires, it is of great importance to have the resources at first hand 
because the number of tasks to be carried out at the same time are numerous, e.g. evacuation, the establishment 
of water supply, locating the fire, assessment for the premises and incident, prepping tools, etc. These tasks are 
to be done in any case of a fire incident, but when a building becomes tall, wide and/or complex, the time scale 
for conducting these tasks change. This also affects the point of when the FRS interrupts the development of the 
fire, and this affects the need for resources. 
 
2.1.2 Before 2005 
Before 2005, the FRS equipment and number of firefighters were based upon a consolidation act by the Ministry 
of the Interior and Health; today, it is based upon a risk assessment. The essential difference is before 2005, an 
FRS was to have a specific amount and type of appliance and service level in proportion of the population. Before 
2005, the FRS were to comply with consolidation act of the municipal FRS configuration (Act nr. 1010 of 
11/12/2002, Historical) (ACT1010). The overall goal is the same, but before 2005, it was described more in 
specific details 
 
Today, the FRS must depart within a certain time, e.g. 1 minute or 5 minutes depending on if it is a full- or part-
time FRS. The § 6 in ACT1010, valid before 2005, required the FRS to arrive on site within: 
1. 10 minutes in areas with high-density houses, and 
2. 15 minutes in areas with low-density housing 
Furthermore, the FRS’s required equipment and appliances depended on the respective number of residents. 
Table 2-1 shows the previous relationship between the number of permanent residents and the number of fire 
units according to §5 in ACT1010. 
One fire unit consisted of two or more appliances, e.g. pump appliance and ladder appliance. According to the 
regulations, the number of first-responding fire units was defined according to the housing type, water supply in 
the area and resident numbers. 
 
 
< 20,000 the FRS consisted of one fire unit 
 
100,000-200,000 the FRS was to have three fire units  
 
>200,000, the number of fire units were to be settled with the local city council

2.2 National Intervention Procedure 
This section presents the result of the conducted interviews with different FRS. The identified differences 
regarding which resources and appliances that are deployed to fires in high-rise buildings in Denmark are 
displayed. 
The chosen FRS all have a high-rise building within the coverage area and consist of full-time or both full-time 
and part-time employed firefighters. Seven different FRS were interviewed, and they cover a wide variety of 
different Danish FRS organizations. A set of standard questions were asked during the interviews, but it was 
quickly identified that these were not always to be answered due to a variety of differences between the 
interviews FRS. It was therefore chosen to establish an understanding of the different FRS and how they each 
handle a fire in a high-rise. On the basis of this understanding, the different intervention procedures displayed 
in section 2.2.2 are written. These interventions descriptions are used for calculations in FBIM later. 
 
In Denmark, there is, in general, no national SOP for intervention in high-rise buildings. FRS’s across Denmark are 
working individually on this subject in order to develop a concept for intervention in high-rise buildings. 
 
2.2.1 Deployed Fire Brigade Resources 
Resources deployed when a fire in a high-rise is reported is different across Denmark. 
Based on the interviews, a variation in approaching a fire in high-rise buildings is identified. The FRS was asked 
what the standard deployed resources were in relation to a signal from an ABA-system, the picklist notification 
via the emergency dispatch center (1-1-2) “Building fire - apartment”, “Building fire - tower block” or “Building 
fire - store”9 and if buildings higher than twenty-two meters were identified as special objects in the system.

In any of the situations in Table 2-3, ISL is in one separate appliance. Need for additional resources will be decided 
upon initial reconnaissance at the scene. Through the interviews, the FRS confirmed, when there was no SOP, 
that the intervention in a high-rise will be similar to a standard “stigrørudlægning,” see section 2.2.2. 
 
In addition to data listed in Table 2-3, it has been possible to find two FRS which have an SOP or concept for fire 
in high-rise buildings solely. The high-rise SOP is not activated upon notification from an ABA-system or sprinkler 
system, but only if the fire is confirmed and the building is confirmed higher than twenty-two meters. It has not 
been possible to collect these SOPs and analyze these in this Master’s thesis. 
 
It is with deliberate intention that no FRS is mentioned by name as the intent of this Master’s thesis is not to 
criticize in any way any FRS in Denmark. The purpose is to state if FBIM can be used to identify the effects of 
different interventions procedures. 
 
2.2.2 Assumed Intervention in High-Rise Buildings in Denmark 
As it only has been possible to identify one Danish SOP for intervention when using a firefighter lift, which also 
was expounded during the interviews, assumptions have been made for calculation purposes only. The assumed 
intervention will be in line with a traditional intervention, as described by DEMA  [10] regarding 
“stigrørudlægninger.” 
 
The sequence of events is described below in section 2.2.2.1 to 2.2.2.4. These sections consist of a diagram 
identifying the intervention step by step in the different phases. Beneath the diagram, notes are made which are 
relevant in relation to intervention and calculation later on. 
 
Below general assumptions and clarifying notes are listed, which apply to all assumed interventions. 
 
“#1” is firefighter number one 
==PAGE:20==
 
 
 
Johanne Salten – S170331 
 
Page 10 of 82 
 
A smoke-helmet team consists of two firefighters as it must due to safety reasons. The two firefighters 
on the team will begin and finish activities at the same time. If one uses air faster than the other during 
search and rescue, both will retreat. This is according to the Danish Working Environment Act, as 
described in “Sikkerhed og sundhed ved røgdygning” [11]. 
 
The areas that need to be searched are the fire compartment and adjacent enclosures, such as the floor 
above fire compartment, etc. If the area that must be searched is larger than what the teams can search 
within the allowed time limit of air use, additional resources are needed to complete the task. 
 
The set-up area is defined as the staircase/lobby one floor beneath the fire compartment floor.

2.3 International Intervention Procedure 
This section describes different international methods and guidelines for intervention in high-rise buildings. In 
the sections, information on the intervention methods from the different countries are described. For this 
Master’s thesis, information from Sweden, Finland and the US was possible to obtain via interviews. The 
interview process and questions are the same as those used for gathering information regarding Danish 
intervention procedures displayed in section 2.2. The first section summarizes the similarity between the 
different international intervention methods and some points of resemblance in the written guidelines. The 
other sections describe the different intervention methods in more detail, which is later used for FBIM 
calculations. The intervention methods are not described as step-by-step in detail. For this information, the 
author refers to the accrual SOP/action card from the FRS of concern. 
 
2.3.1 Summaries – International Intervention Procedures 
The definition of what is considered a high-rise is different from country to country, but it all relates to the 
maximum height of the ladder on the ladder appliance. The intervention methods change when it is no longer 
possible to gain access from the outside through a window to the floor. Buildings which are more than twenty-
three meters high are, in general, considered a high-rise building. 
High-rises can be divided into different categories such as tall, super tall or mega tall buildings. In Denmark, we 
do not have this subdivision yet as it has not been an issue. It has not been possible to distinguish if the 
intervention method changes in collation with the height of a high-rise building, e.g. from a tall (<300meters) to 
mega tall (≥ 600 meters), see Figure 2-3. It has only been possible to distinguish the change in intervention 
method for buildings with a height below or above twenty-three meters. 
 
 
Figure 2-3: Definition on tall, super tall and mega tall buildings. Picture is by courtesy of The Council on Tall Buildings and Urban Habitat. 
http://ctbuh.org/criteria/

The guidelines from the different counties have something in common and point out some of the same issues. 
Below are the common issues listed: 
 
 
It is difficult to create an overview of the floor level of where the incident is taking place due to the 
height 
 
The incident requires a lot of resources/manpower due to the height 
 
Evacuation is difficult due to the number of people 
 
Fallen building parts are a risk 
 
Radio communication, etc., between crew members is difficult and is significant as this is the only way 
of communicating during intervention due to the height 
 
Wind-driven fires are an issue 
 
The guidelines also have the same scope. Below is listed the main context in the different guidelines: 
 
Defines the activities for each arriving appliance in the order of arrival 
 
Defines sections and responsibilities within these 
 
Describes the basic type of construction and installations in these types of buildings 
 
Describes the basic fire service-related installations and the use of these, such as a firefighter lift and 
dry risers

2.3.2 Sweden - Räddningstjänsten Storgöteborg 
Räddningstjänsten Storgöteborg Figure 2-4 have shared their guidelines and 
procedures for when a fire occurs in a high-rise building. 
High-rise building is, in this context, defined as a building where the top floor 
level is twenty-three meters above ground, which is about eight floors high. 
Buildings with a firefighter lift or a pressured dry riser10 are marked as special 
objects in the FRS operational system. This is mostly new buildings. 
When an automatic fire alarm (ABA) is activated in one of these buildings, a 
rescue appliance, a ladder appliance, one captain and six firefighters are 
deployed. If a fire is confirmed, the SOP is activated, three pump appliances, one 
ladder appliance, one water appliance, one OIC, one IC, three captains, and 16 
firefighters are deployed, see Table 2-4. 
Räddningstjänsten Storgöteborg has a guideline for intervention in high-rises 
which describes the tactics. The guideline was written in 2017 in interdisciplinary 
cooperation between Stor-Stockholm fire department, Rescue Service South and Copenhagen Fire Brigade [2]. 
As a supplement for the guideline, some action cards have been made in “easy-to-read” format for the OIC, etc., 
to have with them. These are made to support decision-making during the incident. 
 
2.3.2.1 Guideline for Intervention in High-Rises 
The guideline is written for the incident leaders and is for internal use. The document only deals with a fire in a 
floor above ground, a fire in a basement is coved by another guideline. 
The guideline provides a list of issues that must be considered when there is a high-rise fire. The list points out 
the topics that are different from a low-rise fire. Such as wind-driven fires, water supply, counterflow on the 
stairs, lack of possibilities for overview due to height, it is not possible to identify the relevant floor from the 
outside, suppression methods, risk of fallen building parts, access is only possible via inside the building, etc. 
The guideline provides background knowledge on the building type in relation to: 
 
The types of fire safety installations that can be expected in a high-rise, such as a fireman lift and dry 
riser 
 
A description on how to ventilate gases (wind-driven fires) 
 
Options for safe communication 
 
Staircases  
It also provides a step-by-step description of the tactics for high-rise fires and how to divide the building into 
sections and delegate activities. The plan shows the setup for when an automatic fire alarm is activated and for 
when a fire is confirmed [2]. 
 
2.3.2.2 Guideline for Project Engineering

guideline highlights the issues that are important to address within the building design in relation to securing an 
efficient intervention. 
The guideline describes how the fire service works in the high-rise building, how they use the different fire safety 
installations, the different standards that must be followed in relation to lifts, dry riser and guidelines from the

2.3.3 Finland – Helsinki City Rescue Department 
Helsinki City Rescue Department has a guideline for intervention in a high-rise building 
in general along with other guidelines. The guideline describes not only the tactics but 
also the risk and safety issues, suppression methods, how to evacuate the building 
and the management system of the incident. The guideline is based on and compared 
to similar guidelines from other European and North American countries. 
 
The guideline describes the tactical issues that must be considered when conducting 
a strategic plan for intervention in a high-rise. This could be communications issues, 
issues, how to get access to the building, identifying the fire floor, wind effects up 
high, plus other fluid dynamics challenges that can occur in tall buildings. It also 
outlines the different safety installations that preferably would be installed in a high-
rise and the role they can play when executing an intervention in a high-rise building. 
Figure 2-7: Coat of arms for 
Helsinki City Rescue Department. 
Picture by courtesy of Helsinki 
City Rescue Department. 
==PAGE:30==
 
 
 
Johanne Salten – S170331 
 
Page 20 of 82 
It outlines the different areas the incident area must be divided into and the different roles and responsibilities 
every unit and captain have. It also defines where to ascend to before accessing the fire floor; this will preferably 
be one or two floors beneath the fire floor. 
 
The guideline differs from others as it addresses the evacuation process and the difficulties you may face in 
relation to this. It is suggested that an entire unit is reserved for that activity alone. 
 
The guideline also differs from the others as it does not contain a step-by-step on how to conduct an intervention 
in a high-rise, but it describes all the elements that must be considered and provide background knowledge for 
the OIC to make qualified decisions case-by-case [13]. 
 
The basic resources deployed for a fire in a high-rise of any kind when a confirmed fire are: 
1 x OIC (+1-2 in case of situation) 
3 x pump appliances 
1 x heavy rescue unit 
2 x water appliances 
2 x ladder appliances 
20-28 x firefighters 
1 x captain (2 in case of situation) 
4 x sub-captains 
 
The basic resources deployed when an alarm from an automatic fire alarm system are: 
1 x OIC 
1 x pump appliance 
1 x captain 
5 x firefighters 
 
The guideline is only available in Finnish. The above text is a summary of the guideline, and it was translated by 
the author under no supervision. 
 
2.3.4 USA - Fire Department New York 
Fire Department New York (FDNY), Figure 2-8, has a written firefighting procedure for 
high-rise buildings of a different kind. The one that the author has access to is 
regarding office buildings. 
 
It is divided into different chapters. Chapters one to four describe the purpose, the 
different building types developed over time, ventilation systems in buildings and fire 
pumps. Chapters five to nine describe a tactical plan and considerations, duties for the 
different participants, such as OIC, ladder and engine company. The manual also 
describes an action plan for non-fire emergencies. 
 
The manual’s starting point is major high-rise fires, as the argument is it is easier to 
size down with a plan compared to size up without a plan [14]. 
 
When a fire in a high-rise office building is reported the following are deployed, Table 2-4: 
2 x Battalion Chiefs (OIC+IC) 
4 x ladder company 
4 x engine company

The above is the same whether the notification of fire is from an automatic fire alarm system or a confirmed fire. 
 
In severe incidents, the following will be deployed to support leadership and tactical decisions

1 x Staff Chief 
 
The manual describes that fires in high-rises present significant management, logistical and safety problems due 
to the height. At high-rise fires, consideration of resource availability and response time are important as any 
serious fire will require a large commitment of units and equipment because of extensive logistic problems and 
a need for frequent relief of members. The different initial tasks for the OIC is defined, and its states that the 
verification of the location of the fire is important as it affects all future actions. The OIC must review information 
received from occupants/fire warden/first arriving ladder company. It is not possible for the OIC to identify the 
location of fire by simply looking at the building [14].

3 Method 
This section serves as an introduction to calculation method and the methodic used in this Master’s thesis. 
3.1 Fire Brigade Intervention Model and the use of it 
This section describes FBIM methodology, the philosophy behind it and how it is currently being used in Australia. 
How to apply FBIM and how to calculate intervention time is described in section 3.2. This section serves as an 
isolated qualitative study of FBIM. 
 
3.1.1 FBIM Methodology 
FBIM is the event-based methodology of FRS activities when an incident occurs in buildings. The focus is mainly 
on fire incidents. The model is developed to determine the time from notification to control and extinguishment  
[16] [17]. The model provides an organized decisions-based framework essential to determine and measure FRS 
activities on a timeline basis. The model is the tool available for engineers in Australia when conducting an 
evaluation of the FRS brigade intervention according to building code. This tool is primarily developed in relation 
to performance-based fire safety design. The purpose is to quantify the functional role of the FRS. The model is 
most efficient for determining the required time and resources at the beginning of the event. The model features 
a module for quantifying fire control and extinguishment; however, another method such as thermodynamics, 
etc. could be more suitable for this [16] as more research has been conducted within this field of knowledge. 
 
The method is developed by Australasian Fire and Emergency Service Authorities Council (AFAC) and is used in 
Australia and has been since 2004 [17]. 
 
The model cannot be used to avoid any of them according to the building code required fire safety installations, 
and it is not anticipated that the use of the model will result in any additional fire safety requirements beyond 
those required by the building code. The original purpose of the model is to determine if an intervention is in 
fact possible. If an intervention is not possible, the design must be modified to allow so as required in building 
code. 
 
Many factors are relevant when determining FRS times as they are decision-based actions. The decision is based 
on several parameters such as the building design and its opportunities, the possibility of fire spread, the size of 
the fire, the consequent of the fire, personalities, management techniques, training and experience and the life 
safety of the firefighters, etc. [17]. 
There are several activities that are carried out every time when any incident occurs. These are identified in the 
model and a timeframe has been given for these. This has been done to ensure practical and reasonable results 
are attainable when using FBIM [17]. FBIM can be used as a whole or partly; it depends on the purpose of the 
model [16]. FBIM can be used to determine time for FRS to reach a certain location in the building and be ready 
to do suppression, determining an adequate water flow in relation to the size of dry riser, the necessary 
intervention time during which firefighter tenability and structural stability should be maintained and other 
relations where intervention and building design is coherent. 
 
FBIM consists of 16 individual flowcharts. Each contains a series of events that are undertaken during a fire by 
the FRS. The factors which contribute to FRS intervention and tactics are incorporated into the flowcharts. The 
factors are: 
 
Fire initiation and growth 
 
Time to dispatch and respond 
 
Time to access fire 
 
Information, assessment and strategy determination 
==PAGE:34==
 
 
 
Johanne Salten – S170331 
 
Page 24 of 82 
 
Time to set-up 
 
Time to conduct search and rescue 
 
Exposure protection 
 
Fire control and extinguishment 
 
Environment protection 
For a deeper understanding of what is featured within the above factors, refer to Fire Brigade Intervention Model 
Manual 2.2 [17]. Section 3.2.3 present charts described in relation to the Danish context. 
 
3.1.2 FBIM Philosophy 
FBIM interacts with outputs from other parts of a performance-based fire safety design such as a fire and 
evacuation simulation and analysis. FBIM can be a part of determining the fire safety level within a building. The 
fire safety level is traditionally determined upon the ratio between available safe egress time (ASET) and required 
safe egress time (RSET) [18]. The timeline established in FBIM can be compared with the timeline from the design 
fire scenario and the evacuation model  [16] [17]. The outcome of FBIM will need to be evaluated by the local 
FRS to validate the many input parameters used in the model [16]. The model is a tool for interdisciplinary 
cooperation between the FRS and the consultant during the building design phase. FBIM can also be used by the 
FRS in relation to strategic planning and fire prevention. 
 
The outcome of FBIM depends on two sets of variables: 
1. Externally based variables such as the fire incident development – factor such as fire growth, smoke 
spread, the impact on the building, etc. 
2. Internally based variables such as the effects of the FRS intervention – factors such as limiting the fire 
impact on occupants, adjoining building or property, FRS resources, etc. 
The first set of variables are out of the scope of FBIM and are indirectly regulated by building code requirements, 
e.g. surface requirements in relation to fire, the allowable size of fire compartments and requirements for fire 
safety installation, etc. The second set of variables are within the scope of FBIM and are directly within the control 
of the FRS. 
The two sets of variables together form the fire ground status. The fire ground status is to be understood as what 
the ISL/OIC is facing when arriving on site. As the internal variables are within the control of the FRS, they have 
an opportunity to investigate and secure the necessary resources for FRS intervention in a building [17]. 
 
Once the time of the first-arriving appliance has been determined, it is convenient to compare this to the timeline 
of the fire model. The size of the fire must be addressed as this has an influence on the strategy chosen by the 
ISL/OIC. Time of arrival must be compared to: 
 
Is it before or most likely after flashover? 
 
Has the smoke spread vividly at this time? 
 
Is it necessary for the firefighters to investigate the size of the fire or is it obvious? 
 
Has the fire spread to adjoining buildings or is it most likely to happen soon? 
 
Is the evacuation completed or not? 
This is important as it has a direct influence on the FBIM calculation procedure and the flow through the 
flowcharts, as FBIM is event-based and is an attempt to model decision-based actions [17]. 
 
When the comparison of timelines is done, the rest of the planning can be carried out. There are numerous 
elements that are important in relation to the overall intervention. The strategy and philosophy of FBIM are to 
give the overall strategy and approach to a scenario. FBIM is not intended to determine the time for every event 
==PAGE:35==
 
 
 
Johanne Salten – S170331 
 
Page 25 of 82 
that can occur as some are occurring simultaneously. FBIM can be looked at as a tabletop scenario, where a time 
aspect is added to the fire scenario and working process of the FRS. The fundamental goal is to determine the 
critical path and time for FRS operation. 
 
3.1.3 National Construction Code in Australia and FBIM 
As of National Construction Code 2019 (NCC), [19] FBIM is a part of the NCC in Australia. NCC 2019 is only a 
preview at this writing. The NCC is a performance-based code, as BR18 is in Denmark. The NCC contains 
performance requirements for the construction of buildings. The performance requirements are the minimum 
level which buildings must comply with. The NCC is divided into three volumes. Volume one is requirements for 
multi-residential, commercial, industrial and public buildings and structures, volume two is requirements for 
residential and non-habitable buildings and structures, and volume three is requirements for plumbing and 
drainage for all classes of buildings [20]. 
 
As mentioned, the NCC is a performance-based code, with the performance requirements being the minimum 
level of compliance. This is equivalent to BR18. Performance-based requirements can be met using either a 
performance solution, also called an alternative solution or using a deemed-to-satisfy (DTS) solution, which is a 
pre-accepted solution according to a recipe [20]. 
 
When using an alternative solution, one must use one or more of the assessment methods; the final solution is 
unique for the building. The assessment methods are used to determine if a solution complies with the NCC. The 
methods are located within the general requirements of the NCC [21]. 
There are four methods which can be used; these can also be combined with the method is listed in Table 3-1. 
Table 3-1: Assessment Method Accordingly to the NCC. 
Number 
Method  
Description  
1 
Evidence of Suitability 
Collected evidence according to section A2.2 in NCC which support claims that a martial 
meets the performance or is deemed-to-satisfy requirements. 
2 
Verification Methods 
Results of test, inspection, calculation etc. which determine a solution comply.  
3 
Comparison with the DTS 
A comparative analysis. A solution is e.g. compared the deemed-to-satisfy solution and 
it complies in an equivalent or is superior. 
4 
Expert Judgement 
Judgement by person who has the qualifications and experience necessary to 
determine if a solution meet the requirements 
 
The performance and DTS solution are equivalent to the Danish system, according to BR18 §492. In Denmark, it 
is possible to conduct a fire dimensioning13 where, e.g. fire model and evacuations model is used to conduct a 
comparative analysis or tests. It is also possible to use a pre-accepted solution, as described in the guidelines. 
 
As mentioned, FBIM will be a part of the NCC with the NCC 2019. In relation to this Master’s thesis, it is relevant 
to consider NCC volume one and the relevant guidelines. Section C in volume one is regarding fire resistance and 
section CP 1 to 9 is regarding the performance requirements. Section CP 1, 2 and 9 state structural stability during 
a fire, the spread of fire, and FRS access must be appropriate for fire brigade intervention. Verification methods 
are described in schedule 7 in volume one [19]. Schedule 7 sets out design scenarios which must be considered 
to demonstrate compliance with performance requirements. Design “Scenario FI” is regarding fire brigade 
intervention, and the required outcome is to demonstrate that the fire brigade can undertake fire brigade 
intervention until completion of search and rescue activities and to demonstrate that the level of safety is at 
least equivalent to the DTS. Section 2.8.2 in schedule 7 refers to compliance with the fire scenarios and is 
demonstrated via application of FBIM and the modeling of fire and smoke development [19]. “The international 
fire engineering guideline 2005” [16] refer to FBIM as suitable metrology. 
                                                                
 
13 Brandteknisk dimensionering 
==PAGE:36==
 
 
 
Johanne Salten – S170331 
 
Page 26 of 82 
 
The above mentioned in NCC 2019 corresponds to BR18 §§126-133, but it only states the requirements and not 
how to comply [22]. Currently, there is a guideline for traditional FRS intervention possibilities under 
development. The guideline will apply to traditional buildings; it will provide information regarding necessary 
building elements, e.g. dry riser, requirements for fire lanes and access way for the FRS, etc. The guidelines will 
not describe intervention methods. If a building is not considered traditional, then neither the guideline for 
intervention nor construction is valid; however, these can be used as an underlying basis. In this case, the chosen 
solutions for complying with BR18 §§126-133 must be approved by the board of supervisors14 before issuing 
occupant permit, according to BR18 §23 section 3. The building used in this Master’s thesis is not considered 
traditional as it is above forty-five meters high. 
 
3.2 Calculation Procedure in FBIM 
Basic information regarding using FBIM and parts of the basis for data within FBIM, such as the exercises behind 
the data, tenability criteria, water supply data, etc., are described in this section. A short description of the 
different flowcharts in relation to a Danish context is given. 
 
FBIM is, in this Master’s thesis, used as a quantitative tool for calculating the time of intervention. The results 
will be used in a comparative analysis to identify the effects of the different intervention strategies later on. 
 
At the time of writing, FBIM is a risk-based program that needs to be installed on a computer. It only works with 
Windows 7 or the previous version. But the AFAC will release a new updated online version in the second quarter 
of 2019. It is possible to do an FBIM calculation by hand using tables and flowcharts. 
 
3.2.1 Introduction 
FBIM is a systematic use of 16 flowcharts to calculate the total time required for the FRS to undertake its 
activities. The total FRS intervention—starting from notification of the FRS to extinguishment of the fire—is 
broken down into several components. Each chart represents a distinct component. The charts do not account 
for activities happening concurrently automatically; this needs to be evaluated by the practitioner. Therefore, it 
varies how many charts are necessary for determining the total time of intervention  [16] also the purpose of the 
calculation is important. 
  
When determining the time is important to distinguish between events which occur concurrently or 
consecutively. The total time is not necessarily the successive addition of the individual tasks carried out during 
an incident [17]. 
 
The software is intended for use by the fire service specialist within the FRS and/or fire safety engineers as a part 
of the building design process. The intention is to determine quantitatively the time for FRS intervention and 
allow it to be incorporated into performance-based building designs. FBIM must be used in line with standard 
operational procedures by the relevant FRS. Input and output parameters must be reviewed by the relevant FRS. 
The software has been developed as a design tool and is not meant to represent the real-life activities of 
firefighters attending a fire  [17] [23].  
                                                                
 
14 Kommunalbestyrelsen 
==PAGE:37==
 
 
 
Johanne Salten – S170331 
 
Page 27 of 82 
3.2.2 The Basic Strategy of Applying FBIM 
As mentioned, FBIM in relation to a fully performance-based solution cannot standalone. The model requires 
input data from the fire and evacuation models as this influent on the path through the charts. 
As an example, if the time for complete evacuation exceeds the time of arrival, the fire brigade needs to focus 
on search and rescue rather than control and extinguish. In Denmark, the FRS is not as a standard a part of the 
primary evacuation according to the preliminary guideline for traditional intervention methods15, unless agreed 
otherwise.  
The conditions at the incident scene are important as they determine the appropriate FRS decision and activities. 
The conditions are defined as the output data from the fire and evacuation models at the time of arrival of the 
FRS [16]. 
 
The procedure must therefore be: 
1. Define the development of the fire scenario, thus calculation detection time, etc. 
2. Define the evacuation time for the above fire scenario 
3. Define the time of arrival of the FRS including time for notification etc. 
4. Compare the time of arrival with fire scenario development 
5. Define the next most logical FRS activity and follow the chart from there, the next move should depend 
on if the FRS must focus on evacuation, rescue or suppression 
 
 
                                                                
 
15 The guideline is under development and have been in hearing but is not yet publish. “Vejledning til Redningsberedskabets indsatsmuligheder”.  
==PAGE:38==
 
 
 
Johanne Salten – S170331 
 
Page 28 of 82 
3.2.3 Review of Charts 
Below, the 16 FBIM flowcharts are reviewed and related to a Danish context. For a detailed review of each 
flowchart, refer to the Fire Brigade Intervention Model Manual Version 2.2 [17]. 
Each chart is a decision tree, where the colored squares indicate a time-contributing activity. Decisions are 
symbolized by a diamond and consist of a yes/no question. The total time for each chart is the sum of the 
colored boxes along the chosen path. Whether or not the time contributes to the total time of intervention 
depends on the activity and end time of other concurrent activities. The charts in the next sections are also to 
be found in Appendix A. 
 
Figure 3-1 shows the primary flow and relationships 
between all the charts in general. Chart 1 to 4 often 
happens sequentially at the beginning of an event. As 
additional resources are needed, charts 2-4 will happen 
concurrently with other activities. Charts 5-12 often 
happen concurrently depending on the situation. Charts 
13-16 can happen concurrently, but it depends on 
available resources and what is required of the FRS. 
Depending on the situation and available resources, 
charts 13 or 15 can happen concurrently and is most likely 
to happen after chart 12. 
Figure 3-1: Primary flow thought chart and the 
relationship between them [16]. 
==PAGE:39==
 
 
 
Johanne Salten – S170331 
 
Page 29 of 82 
 
Figure 3-2: Flowcharts in order and in relation to Danish procedures. 
In Figure 3-2, there are the 12 flowcharts used in this Master’s thesis shown in order and which are linked. The 
button line distinguishes which happen sequentially and concurrently. Red charts are activities that must be 
done before the next activity can begin; it can either be due to available resources or due to concurrent 
==PAGE:40==
 
 
 
Johanne Salten – S170331 
 
Page 30 of 82 
reasons. These activities must follow in a certain order. Blue charts are activities that are connected to a red 
activity and is performed during a red chart. Blue is only relevant if the outcome of the previous red chart 
activity requires it. Blue charts add time to the total time for a red chart. Orange charts are activities that can 
be performed during a red/blue chart. Depending on resources, the activities must be done before continuing 
to the next red activity. 
A dotted line symbolizes when the critical path can take two different paths depending on available resources. 
A solid line symbolizes when activities must be performed in a certain order. The top timeline refers to 
intervention strategies presented in section 2.2.2. Figure 3-2 is developed in relation to this specific Master’s 
thesis, and it can therefore not be seen as a general perception on how to apply FBIM. 
 
3.2.3.1 Chart 1 – Time Taken for Initial Brigade Notification 
 
 
 
Chart 1 calculates the time taken to notify the FRS, see Figure 3-3. The timeline starts when the fire is detected 
and ends when the notification reaches the FRS technical dispatch system. The chosen path through the chart

depends on if an automatic detection- or suppression system is installed, or if notification, in any case, has to be

A fire/smoke detector and other system parts can have system-delays16. Without prior agreement, this

delay due the connection between the ABA-system and the FRS central system.

Chart 2 calculates the time taken to dispatch the FRS and other needed resources, see Figure 3-4. The timeline 
starts when the notification of a fire reaches the FRS dispatch system and ends when the FRS leaves the station 
to proceed to the incident scene [17]. 
 
The chosen path through the chart depends on how the call or notification reaches the FRS, the dispatch system 
used by the FRS and the staffing. It depends on if the fire is call into the local fire station or if is it an automatic 
signal. In Denmark, it is not possible to call in a fire at the local fire station. The signal/call can either be through 
the emergency dispatch center (1-1-2 centralen) or an automatic signal from a specific building. 
 
The staffing of the FRS station is also of importance. The “sent and receive” process via a pager can take more 
time if the station is staffed by part-time firefighters compared to a full-time staffed station with a call-out 
system. 
 
Chart 2 also involves chart 3, as this chart accounts for the time for leaving the station. In the calculations time 
contributions from chart 2 and 3 is based on times given in FBIM tables, but the activities are concurrently with 
chart 4. 
 
3.2.3.3 Chart 3 – Time Taken for Firefighters to Respond to Dispatch Call 
 
 
Chart 3 calculates the time taken for firefighters to respond to dispatch call and be ready to leave the station, 
see Figure 3-5. The timeline starts when the dispatch call reaches the firefighters and ends when the firefighters 
are in the appliance ready to leave the station. The chart accounts for travel time to the station if the station is 
staffed by part-time firefighters, time to get dressed and become mobile [17]. 
 
Important time in relation to a Danish context is described in section 3.2.3.2 and 3.2.3.4.

Chart 4 calculates the time taken to reach the fire scene, see Figure 3-6. The timeline starts when the appliance 
leaves the station and ends when it has reached the scene. The chart accounts for travel time through different 
areas. This could be the inner-city center or a rural area [17]. 
Different calculation methods are presented which could be of interest for Danish FRS. In Denmark, the maximum 
allowable time of response is often agreed upon between the FRS and the city council, see explained in section 
2.1. The interesting matter is if it is possible to comply if the city roads change, if the alarm comes during rush-
hour or when the city expands. In the calculations

requirements for leaving the station is as described in section 2.1.2. The allowed time to response starts when 
the notification is received by the FRS and includes time to dress, leave the station and drive. In the calculations 
time contribution from chart 4 is set to be as previous time limits, as described in section 2.1.2.

3.2.3.5 Chart 5 – Time Taken for Initial Determination of Fire Location 
 
 
Chart 5 calculates the time taken to assess the building, localize the point of information, e.g. the fire information 
panel (FIP) and time to move appliance if needed, see Figure 3-7. The timeline starts with identifying if travel is 
needed within the site, which can be relevant if dealing with large premises. The timeline also identifies the state 
of occupation, if the fire is visual upon arrival, and time taken to communicate with fire warden. The chart also 
addresses if forced entry or security procedures are necessary. The timelines end with the time taken to find the 
location of the fire [17]. The chart plays an important role as it identifies the time taken to locate the fire. In risk 
assessment described in section 2.1, only the time taken to reach the kerbside is addressed. But it is important 
to identify if it is possible to reach the location of the fire when also dealing with security procedures, locked 
doors, complex building layout (wayfinding), reaching and understanding the FIP and gathering information from 
fire warden/resource person. In Denmark the FIP corresponds to an ABA control panel. Chart 5 also identifies 
the benefit of having a pre-fire planning document. A pre-fire planning document is part of the operational 
preparedness; it involves assessing the site for a potential fire, and planning strategies to deal with an incident. 
This can be beneficial as it can be expected to be a time saver for when an incident occurs.

3.2.3.8 Chart 8 – Time Taken to Travel to Set-up Area 
 
 
Chart 8 calculates the time taken to travel to the set-up area, see Figure 3-10. A set-up area is in FBIM defined as 
the area where equipment is located; it can be next to where appliances are parked, or where an advanced 
equipment depot is located [17]. Travel time includes time to travel within the site, e.g. if the premises is very 
large, such as an industrial building site. Travel time also includes time taken for security procedures and internal 
travel within the building and site. Chart 8 is connected to charts 6 and 9. 
In relation to high-rise fires, the set-up area would be one or to floor below the fire compartment. The chart also 
addresses the matter of if the building is evacuated or not before intervention is begun. If a building is not 
evacuated prior to intervention, counterflow in access routes must be accounted for. 
 
Time taken to travel to set-up area becomes important in connection with high-rise buildings, as the time is taken 
to ascend and descend increase with the height and if the building is not evacuated prior to entering. This can 
have an impact on the total time for suppression and intervention. Pre-planning can be of interest in these cases, 
as charts 7 and 8 can optimize time spent on reconnaissance, traveling and gathering equipment. 
 
If primary evacuation is not completed when the FRS arrive, some FRS resources should be assigned to the 
activity. This should be considered when the fire safety level of a building is evaluated in relation to a 
performance-based fire safety design as this change the primary FRS-focus on arrival.  
For fire safety consultants, chart 8 is of interest as it asks important questions in relation to access control 
systems. Access control systems must be designed in such a way that intervention is possible and unnecessary 
delays are avoided. If it is not possible to avoid these due to the use of the building, agreement upon it must be 
made with the local FRS during building design development.

In the calculations chart 8 and 9 is used to calculate the time taken for firefighters to travel from the ground for 
floor to the fire compartment ready to conduct search and rescue.  
3.2.3.9 Chart 9 – Time Taken for Firefighter Travel 
 
 
Chart 9 calculates travel time in relation to chart 8, see Figure 3-11. Chart 9 address objects which could hinder 
access, such as locked doors and time to vertical/horizontal travel within the building [17]. Vertical and horizontal 
travel can either be via stair, lift or a combination thereof. 
 
The chart is of interest as it identifies important objects which influence on the building design and fire 
intervention. A common risk assessment carried out by the FRS, as mentioned in section 2.1, mostly identify the 
time taken to arrive at the kerbside. Time taken to gain entry is in some cases of as the same importance. As 
buildings become more complex, time to gather information and locate the origin of the fire should be accounted 
for. The fire develops simultaneously during this period of time. It is also of importance if the building is occupied 
or not, e.g. if a fire occurs outside openings hours FRS access options change. The incident can become severe 
due to difficult access options for the FRS. The building owner or user should in some cases have an operating

organization which can support the FRS in case of a fire. This can be of great importance if the building houses 
an industrial enterprise. The chart can also identify the effects of available firefighter lifts vs. stairs.

3.2.3.11 Chart 11 – Time taken to Set Up Water Supply Requirements 
 
 
Chart 11 calculates the time taken to set up water supply with enough capacity and pressure to undertake 
prolonged search and rescue, fire control and extinguishment, etc., see Figure 3-12. The charts both describe 
internal hydrant systems within buildings which are attached to local water supply, external hydrant systems on-
site and if no hydrant system is available. Times include the time is taken to connect, layout and charge hoses. 
The need for additional resources is also addressed, which involves chart 2 etc. [17]. 
 
As buildings become more complex, it could be necessary to identify the time taken to re-position appliances, 
layout hoses, the type of water supply, the possible needed amount of water, etc. The needed amount of water 
corresponds to the size of the fire. The size of the fire is theoretically determined by a fire model; a fire, in reality, 
can develop beyond the results from the fire model. But if accounted for within the performance-based fire 
design and district planning, water supply system or similar, e.g. enough water appliance within the FRS can be 
established. 
In connection with district planning, identifying the need for water is of interest as it can have an impact on either 
installation of new fire hydrants in the area, a reorganization of water appliance within the FRS or a need to add 
new water appliance.

In the calculations chart 11 is used to account for time spent on setting up water supply from a fire hydrant to

3.2.3.12 Chart 12 – Time Taken for Search and Rescue 
 
 
Chart 12 calculates the time taken for firefighters to undertake search and rescue within smoke-filled areas and 
adjacent enclosures, see Figure 3-14. The time is calculated under specific assumptions. It is assumed only smoke-
filled compartments will be searched. Therefore, it is assumed occupants are safe if located in other parts of the 
building which are kept tenable due to fire and smoke control. The time for search and rescue is necessary to 
calculate if the FRS are assumed to secure evacuation from the building if evacuation is ongoing when the FRS 
arrive at the kerbside, or if evacuation involves many people, e.g. in connection with malls, sports arenas, and 
Figure 3-14: Chart 12. 
==PAGE:51==
 
 
 
Johanne Salten – S170331 
 
Page 41 of 82 
other public assembly locations. FBIM assumes a search and rescue team consisting of three firefighters. In 
Denmark, a team would consist of two. 
The chart address the need for resources to conduct search and rescue if the required resources are available if 
firefighter tenability criteria in relation to the fire have been exceeded and time of BA limit [17]. BA time limit 
relates to the amount of air available for the search and rescue team and if they can continue or the team must 
be replaced. 
 
Firefighter tenability criteria are not accounted for in Denmark. The tenability criteria in relation to firefighters 
are described in section 3.2.5. Within the performance-based design, it is common to base ASET on tenability 
criteria for occupants, but it is not common to analyses further. The fire model should also include the state of 
the fire at the time of FRS arrival. It is often assumed that the FRS will extinguish the fire upon arrival, but if the 
fire is to develop it might not be possible for the firefighter to enter the building. The bunker gear has limits to 
which it can withstand heat. Section 3.2.5 and Table 3-3 describe bunker gear limits. It can be of much relevance 
to perform this analysis if the building is tall, wide or complex. 
 
In the calculations chart 12 is used to calculate time for search and rescue in relation the number of available 
smoke-helmet teams.

The chart could gain more ground within the FRS in connection with risk assessment, as it handles fire spread to

The chart is most relevant for consultants if a building project consists of more than one building on the premises,

3.2.3.15 Chart 15 – Time Taken to Control and Extinguish the Fire 
 
 
Chart 15 calculates the time taken to control and extinguish the fire, see Figure 3-15. Time taken to set up the

other extinguishing media is applied, the fire will continue growing [17]. 
 
In relation to the performance-based design, the chart could indicate if the dry riser system is adequate. As 
described in section 3.2.6, the dry riser system was not adequate in the Liverpool Carpark fire as it was not 
possible to provide the needed amount of water on the floor of which the fire was located. This issue becomes 
interesting in relation to high-rise buildings, as the dry riser can be the only water supply option for suppression 
in the building.

3.2.4 Data Within FBIM  
Within FBIM, several tables are given with suitable times for events carried out by the FRS during an intervention. 
These times is the result of a profound data collection involving data from the literature review, training 
practices, statistical collation methods and Delphi methods (experts’ knowledge) [17]. 
 
The data have been gathered, evaluated and interpreted statistically. The mean time and standard deviations for 
sub-activities are showed in the tables within the FBIM computer program and manual. The data is collected in 
Australia. 
 
The data for FRS activities realis to some extent on eight different proforma exercises. Each exercise is described 
in detail in the “Fire Brigade Intervention Manual 2.2” [17] and in “Assessment and Validation of the Fire Brigade  
Intervention Model for use within New Zealand and Performance-Based Fire Engineering” [25]. Exercises have 
been modified for the purpose of logging time for individual activities.

3.2.5 Firefighter Tenability Criteria 
Within FBIM, sets of tenability criteria for firefighters have been introduced. These are a part of flow chart 12, 
which address the search and rescue activities [17]. It is important to address whether it is safe and possible for 
the firefighters to enter the building/apartment/room where the origin of the fire is. If it is not safe, the tactic 
changes and the fire is dealt with from there. 
The tenability criteria for firefighters are more aggressive compared to those for occupants. The parameters are 
the same. You could argue that firefighters are protected by bunker gear and BA, but the bunker gear has its 
limits, and so does the human body [29]. 
The tenability criteria in FBIM derive from a research report done by the UK Home Office Fire Research and 
Development Group in 1994, Research Report Number 61 [30]. 
The criteria are divided into groups of work conditions and address times for which a firefighter and bunker gear 
can withstand the different parameters. The tenability criteria are shown in Figure 3-19.

performance-based fire designs in general. Guideline for safety and health of firefighter “Sikkerhed og sundhed 
ved røgdygning” [11], state that bunker gear protection level, in general, provide protection for five minutes at 
250 ֯C. Tenability criteria are not used in the calculation, as no fire model is produced, in relation to chart 12 it is 
assumed that the tenability criteria are not exceeded.   
 
3.2.6 Water Supply Assessment 
FBIM address the issues with water and whether it is sufficient. This is important as water is often the chosen 
fire extinguisher media when dealing with fires. Other fire extinguishing media, such as foam of various kinds 
exist, but the FBIM only deals with water. 
Flowchart 10 and 11 address the issue with setting up of water supply for firefighting. Flowchart 15 addresses  
the issue by stating how much water is needed in relation to the size of the fire. Both issues need to be addressed 
because if it is not possible to provide enough water, it most likely not possible to extinguish the fire [17]. 
 
To draw a parallel, this issue was of importance in the Kings Dock Car Park Fire 31st December 2017, also known 
as the Liverpool Car Park Fire. The number of the available dry riser, the water flow these could provide, and the 
size of the fire did not match [32]. Hence, it was impossible for the first firefighter crew to extinguish the fire. 
The building was designed in accordance with Approved Document B [33], this is the English counterpart to the 
Danish fire safety guidelines as the “Eksempelsamligen om brandsikring af byggeri”  [34] and the new guidelines 
for pre-accepted solutions with regard to BR18. The guidelines do address the water supply issue, but only to the 
extent that, e.g., a dry riser must be provided. 
 
The calculation of sufficient water supply in FBIM is based on the method described in “Report 1014 – An 
Engineering Approach to Fire-Fighting Tactics by Stefan Särdqvist” [35]. To extinguish the fire, energy from the 
fire must be absorbed. This is done by applying water as water evaporates at 100 [ ֯C], and therefore there is a 
change in state. A change in state requires energy, which is taken from heat from the fire. When enough energy 
is absorbed, the fire will be extinguished. The heat absorption capacity of water can theoretically be calculated 
as below, see equation 1 to 4: 
 
To heat water from 10 ֯C to 100 ֯C, the required energy is: 
Q = c ∙ m ∙ ∆T
