𝑘𝑔] ∙3) ∙0.2 = 17 [𝑀𝐽] = 17 [𝑀𝑊] > 4 [𝑀𝑊]  
 
(5) 
 
Water supply is not analyzed further in this Master’s thesis, it is only addressed sketchy in relation to the need 
amount to fill the dry riser compared with the available amount on the pump appliance. 
3.2.7 Step-by-Step Calculation 
The international fire engineering guideline [16] describes the calculation procedure step by step. This is 
explained below in Figure 3-20, and this will be used later in this Master´s thesis [16], [17]. Times in each chart 
are calculated individually. If a chart refers to another chart, the timer from the second chart is added to the first 
chart. Which charts occur concurrently have to be reviewed and decided afterwards and incorporated via start 
time of each chart. 
 
 
Figure 3-20: Calculation procedure for the use of FBIM.  
 
Step 1 through 6 have been used in this Master’s thesis in relation with calculations. The order of the charts in 
any case is to be found in Appendix E and Appendix F. 
STEP 1
• Notification
• Determine the time of notification of the fire brigade. This time can either be time for detection by the fire alarm system or a phone call. This time must include any delay if any exist within the system.
• Flowchart 1
STEP 2
• Dispatch
• Determine time for fire brigade to dispatch fire station. This includes time to get dressed, etc. for firefighters.
• Flowchart 2 and 3
STEP 3
• Arrival
• Determine time of arrival. This cover the accrual movement on the road.
• Flowchart 4
STEP 4
• Investigation
• Determine the time for OIC to conduct investigation and information search. Have timeline from fire scenario and evacuation in mind.
• Flowcharts 5, 6, 7 and perhaps 9
STEP 5
• Set up
• Determine time for set-up at ”kerbside”.
• Flowcharts 6, 8, 9, 10 and 11
STEP6
• Search and rescue
• Determine time for search and rescue if needed, compare to evacuation model or SOP.
• Flowchart 12
STEP 7
• Fire Attack
• Determine time for commencement of fire attack. Have fire development in mind as this can be to protect adjoining buildings instead of extinguishing the fire within the building.
• Flowcharts 13 and 15
STEP 8
• Determine the effect suppression activities, compare to fire development curve
• Flowchart 15.
STEP 9
• End time reached?
• Determine if the end time has been reached – is the fire extinguished and/or are more activities to done.
• Flowcharts 14 and 16
STEP 10
• End time

4 Case Description and Calculation Basis 
This section describes all necessary information needed for calculation purposes. Section 4.1 describes facts 
regarding building geometry and fire safety measures. Section 4.2 describes the notification of the FRS and which 
information is available. Section 4.3 is regarding delimitation for the calculations. Section 4.5 is a description of 
all the assumptions made in relation to complete the FBIM calculations. These assumptions must be made to 
find a way through the flowcharts. 
4.1 Building Facts 
The building used in the calculations is an early design proposal. It is 38 floors high, and the top-level floor is 
139.6 meters above the ground floor, total height is 144 meters. The total area is 38.000 m2 (New building), the 
old building at ground floor is preserved as shown in Figure 4-1. The building is used for multiple purposes; at 
the top level is a restaurant and the ground floor is a lobby. The floors in between are used for hotel, residential 
and offices. 
In relation to fire safety, the following fire safety measures are being incorporated in the building: 
 
Dry riser with additional pump is installed to secure water supply on all floors; valves are assumed closed 
when not in use 
 
An automatic fire alarm system (ABA) with a commander-room is installed with detection on all floors. 
An ABA-system’s purpose is to detect a possible fire at an early stage and notify the FRS and activate 
the AVA. 
 
An automatic warning system (AVA) is installed on all floors with two-way communication option. The 
warning system is adjusted in relation to occupancy and zone-evacuation plan. The purpose of the 
warning system it to alert occupants at an early stage of a possible fire and to provide the best 
opportunities for evacuation 
 
An automatic water sprinkler system (AVS) is installed on all floors adjusted to usage category. The 
purpose of the AVS system is to prevent fire spread and possibly extinguishing the fire at an early stage 
 
As only one staircase is provided lift for evacuation is installed. These are safe to use during a fire 
 
The one staircase (fire tower) and lobby on every floor is pressurized. This is to ensure that the area is 
smoke-free both for evacuation and intervention 
 
A firefighter lift is provided for FRS. The purpose is to ease and provide good interventions opportunities 
for the FRS 
 
The load-bearing construction has a two-hour fire rating, this is based on the overall fire safety level, a 
CFD and evacuation analysis. All floors are to be considered primary fire compartment separations 
 
30th floor

Alarm notification: 
ABA - pick list: Building fire - tower block20 
 
Below are the circumstances regarding the situation the FRS meet upon arrival: 
 
The building is fully occupied 
 
There are no fire wardens in the building 
 
No special pre-fire planning is made for the building by the FRS, except O-plans 
 
Evacuation is ongoing when the FRS arrive. The floor beneath and above the fire compartment is warned 
via the ABA- and AVA-system and evacuated automatically  
 
Not all who are normally in the open office area are yet accounted for when the FRS arrive 
 
Office-employees report to the FRS smoke that has been seen in the kitchenette 
Time of the day and year are not important in this context as it does not have an influence on the calculations. 
 
4.3 Delimitation 
The calculation only includes charts 1-12. These charts are important in relation to clarifying if a certain 
intervention method has an effect compared to others. These are also important in relation to clarify the need 
for instantly available resources at the incident scene at the beginning of the intervention as if the resources are 
not available, the intervention time will most likely be prolonged. 
Activities addressed in charts 13-16 are important, but for solving these activities, requests for additional FRS 
units have been possible and therefore due to the construction of the FRS system in Denmark, the needed 
resources should be available, see section 2.1. It is assumed charts 1-12 represent the initial intervention21 and 
charts 13-15 represent the final intervention22. 
It is chosen not to calculate the actual time spent on driving from the fire station to the scene as fire stations can 
be relocated over time. Therefore, it is not reasonable to base a fire safety level on an accurate location of the 
fire station in relation to a building. The time of arrival must be based on the time agreed upon with the city 
council, see section 2.1. Arrival times should consider delays caused by rush-hour traffic or similar.

4.5 Calculations Assumptions 
Below are the assumptions used in the calculations listed. The number written in the note in the calculations 
refers to this list. 
 
Several factors are relevant when determining the time to complete FRS activities. The method used in FBIM is 
complex as it is an attempt to model a decision-based process and actions. It must cover a broad spectrum of 
possibilities, personalities, management techniques and systems, training, available resources, etc. For this 
reason, activities and consequences have been simplified to ensure practical and reasonable results are 
attainable [17]. 
Therefore, the assumptions are listed below are based on information gathered through interviews with the FRS 
and guidelines for intervention management23. Booklet “Indsatsledelse på skadestedet” [37] and “Retningslinjer 
for indsatsledelse” [38] are used. 
 
As described in section 4.2, not all persons are accounted for when the FRS arrive. The first priority for the FRS 
is, therefore, to determine if people are in danger as defined in “Indsatsledelse på skadestedet” [37] and in §12 
in “Beredskabsloven” [39]. Therefore, it is assumed firefighter resources are put into action toward search and 
rescue and not helping with the evacuation. 
  
4.5.1 Assumption Made in Relation to Flowcharts 
List numbers are to be read in line with the calculations. 
1. Times in chart 1 are based upon times set out in DBI guideline 232 [24]. Alarm signal from one smoke 
detector or sprinkler head/flow switch can be suppressed up to 60 seconds for smoke detectors and 30 
seconds for flow switches. Time for a signal to reach the FRS alarm system can take up to 10 seconds if 
the connection is wired. If the connection is wireless, it can take 20-60 seconds depending on if the 
system is required by law or not. 
                                                                
 
23 Indsatsledelse 
==PAGE:64==
 
 
 
Johanne Salten – S170331 
 
Page 54 of 82 
 
2. The building is assumed to located in the inner city and response time used in calculations is assumed 
to be 10 minutes (600 [s]) if full-time firefighters and 15 minutes (900 [s]) if part-time firefighters, see 
section 2.1. Danish time of response for FRS starts when the call is received; therefore, the time includes 
time to dress. It is chosen to use a static time frame rather than real driving time as it is not ways and 
customs in Denmark to favor a building in fire safety matters if the fire station is located within x 
kilometers from the building. 
 
3. ABA-panel (FIP) is in a dedicated room (point C in Figure 4-2), this is the commander-room. Therefore, 
the time for internal travel to primary information target (chart 9 under chart 5) is only dependent on 
the distance from “kerbside” (point A in Figure 4-2) to commander-room and access-door type. The 
door is most likely a side-hung door. Only the ISL should enter the commander-room when first arriving 
if not otherwise specified, which is relevant in chart 5. The two-way communication system is in the 
commander-room. 
 
4. It is assumed that activities in charts 6 and 7 are carried out simultaneously as activities in chart 6 are 
done by firefighters, and activities in chart 7 are done by ISL or ISL/HL. 
 
5. BA is inside the appliance and donned before stepping out of the appliance. Only masks must be donned 
just before entering fire compartment or similar. Therefore, the time is zero. 
 
6. A firefighter team in the calculations is assumed to consist of at least two firefighters, except for the 
task “connecting fire hose to fire hydrant.” This task only requires one firefighter. Therefore, time to 
gather tools is only dependent on the longest time as other tools are being gathered simultaneously. 
 
7. It is assumed in relation to chart 7, that the OIC in a Danish context corresponds to ISL. Therefore, the 
ISL walks to the fire compartment. This is assumed as it is standard procedure according to 
“Indsatsledelse på skadestedet” [37], it is the ISL who is responsible for determining if people are in 
direct danger, the stage of the fire and if the fire has or can spread. This is a conservative assumption, 
as most likely the ISL will not walk to the fire compartment. But when it is not otherwise specified as 
done in an SOP, this assumption must be taken as it is described in standard procedures. 
If an SOP state HL is a part of the initial reconnaissance, it is assumed HL will walk to the fire 
compartment on the 30th floor with equipment and two firefighters as described in the SOP for the use 
of firefighter lift, see section 2.2.2.4. 
 
8. Location of appliances is possible just outside the building (point A in Figure 4-2). Point A is in chart 7 
defined as the outside set up area for FRS. Therefore, repositioning of the appliance is not needed and 
therefore the time set to zero.  
 
9. Dry riser connecting point is located near the entrance point (point E in Figure 4-2). Distance between 
the appliance and dry riser connecting point is equal or less than 10 meters as according to Danish Fire 
Safety Guidelines [34]. 
 
10. In chart 10, the initial water supply must be set up, which is assumed to be a hose from pump appliance 
to dry riser. When only a pump appliance is deployed, the amount of water is limited. The amount is 
only enough to fill the dry riser and hoses. When the amount of water is limited a more secure water 
supply must be set up before firefighters enter the building. Therefore, chart 11 must be completed 
before continuing the intervention. If resources allow. its tasks can be performed simultaneously. 
 
==PAGE:65==
 
 
 
Johanne Salten – S170331 
 
Page 55 of 82 
11. The fire hydrant is available on site. It is located outside the premises. The distance is chosen to 40 
meters by the author as FBIM data indicate if the distance is above 30-meter time to search for fire 
hydrant takes three times as long as if the hydrant is within sight. It is most likely a fire hydrant is not 
with insight as these, over time, have been reduced by the municipality and FRS due to suppression 
techniques [40]. 
 
12. It is assumed that firefighter tenability criteria have not been exceeded in relation to chart 12 as no CFD 
analysis is performed. 
 
13. In chart 12, the searched area in the building is assumed larger than the area which is initially identified 
as smoke filled. This means the whole fire compartment have been searched. 
 
14. In chart 12 when adjacent enclosures must be searched, the only primary search is accounted for as it 
is assumed smoke-helmet team can walk through the area and have a clear vision. 
 
15. The normal entrance for the building is used by the FRS to gain access to stairs and lifts (point B in Figure 
4-2). The door is assumed to be a side-hung door. Firefighter lift is in the center of the building next to 
regular lifts and the staircase (point D1 and D2 in Figure 4-2). Distance from lift or staircase on the floor 
is assumed to be 6 meters as it then complies with Danish Fire Safety Guidelines [34]. 
 
16. Time to resolve wayfinding in chart 5 is assumed to cover ISL finding the red flash mounted on the facade 
just outside the commander-room. Red flash must be installed in order to comply with installations 
guideline “DBI Retningslinje 232” [24]. Ten seconds is chosen as the area outside the building is 
comparable to a single story, open plan as listed in table K in FBIM data, see Appendix D.  
 
17. In chart 8, the times for internal travel are not adjusted for hindrance factors such as counterflow on 
stairs. It is assumed the width of the stair is 1.2 meters as it then complies with Danish fire safety 
guideline [34]. The Danish fire safety guideline suggests if a stair flight is 1.2 meters it is wide enough to 
be used simultaneal for evacuation and intervention. 
 
18. The calculation ends after the initial intervention is completed which is when search and rescue are 
over. The calculation includes chart 1 to 12. In chart 12 it is chosen to assume the searched area have 
exceeded the smoke -logged area and there for end the calculation. This gives a total time for searching 
the fire compartment. This time is used to give an estimation on needed search sessions and thus a 
possible need for resources as described in section 4.5.2. 
 
19. In boxes 7 and 8 in chart 7, it is the first time the need for additional resources is addressed in the 
flowcharts. This is just after ISL have conducted initial reconnaissance. It is assumed ISL will request 
additional resources at this moment. It is defined in standard procedures in “Indsatsledelse på 
skadestedet” [37] that ISL must request for additional resources as soon as the need is identified. It 
would most likely be done prior to entering the building, therefore is the assumption conservative. 
 
20. As the fire is in a high-rise on the 30th floor, it is assumed that it is not possible to directly identify the 
location of the fire by just looking at the building, no smoke is coming out through the windows, etc. 
This could might well be the case for low-rise buildings. High-rise buildings are under some 
circumstances not equipped with openable windows due to the function of ventilation systems and 
wind effects. The assumptions are found to be reasonable as interventions guidelines address this to be 
an issue in relation to high-rise fires, see sections 2.3.2 and 2.3.4 
 
==PAGE:66==
 
 
 
Johanne Salten – S170331 
 
Page 56 of 82 
4.5.1.1 Calculation – Sweden  
There is only one calculation conducted in relation to the Swedish SOP, as the described options in section 2.3.2.3 
are upscale of each other. Option one is chosen as the foundation for the calculation as the calculation is about 
a signal from an ABA signal and the initial intervention. At the scene, fire is confirmed, and option two is put in 
to use when additional resources are requested. As option two is an upscale option one, no change is made the 
step-by-step procedure fire unit 1 is performing during the initial intervention. Therefore, it is reasonable to do 
one calculation as the purpose is to determine effects during the initial intervention phase.  
The time for search and rescue is chosen not to divide by 1,5 even though fire unit 3 will be available on site 
when search and rescue are begun. The SOP describes fire unit 3 as a replacement team fire unit 1, and therefore 
is it not possible to have two teams working side by side.  
Fire unit 2 is to ensure stairwell, lift shaft and floors above the fire compartment is free of smoke. If necessary, 
fire unit 2 assists evacuation.  
 
Time for notification, dispatch, and response is assumed to be as pr. Danish calculation, FBIM data is assumed 
valid. This is to provide a standard of comparison for calculations.  
 
4.5.1.2 Calculation – FDNY 
There is only one calculation conducted in relation to the FDNY intervention procedure as the response is the 
same. 
The intervention procedure is put in to use in a Danish context, which poses a challenge as intervention 
procedures often reflect and match building designs traditions. This is expressed in relation to setting up water 
supply. High-rises in NY must have a riser system which is connected to a pump system, and if filled with water 
prior to intervention without help from the FRS [14]. The pumps must be operated by the fire warden in the 
building. Therefore, there is no responding pump appliance initial task to set up the water supply other than the 
initial. The first-responding pump appliance is to set up a connection to the water supply system, but only as an 
additional supply for sprinklers and risers. 
It is chosen to assume first-arriving pump appliance is responsible for setting up complete water supply as per 
the rest of the calculations. This gives an artificial result in relation to the timer for setting up of water supply, 
but as the goal is to determine the time to reach the fire compartment and time for search and rescue which is 
conducted by the ladder appliance team, it has no effect on the results which is compared with other calculations. 
 
FBIM data is assumed valid and conservative for this calculation as standard FBIM crew size is less then FDNY 
crew size. The time for notification, dispatch, and response are assumed to be as pr. Danish calculation, FBIM 
data is assumed valid. This is to provide a standard of comparison for calculations. 
 
4.5.2 Firefighter Air Use and Work Time 
When calculating the time for search and rescue, the use of air by the smoke-helmet team must be accounted 
for as it determines for how long a team can perform search and rescue. This is what FBIM calls a BA limit. 
The use of air is calculated as listed in Table 4-2 according to “Elevhæfte – Indsats – Vejrtrækningen, Røggasser, 
Kroppens reguleringsmekanismer” [41]. It is assumed that an air pressure bottle contains 6 [L] of air at 300 [bar] 
pressure. The chosen type of bottle is typical type, and as the size is relatively small it is a conservative assumption 
a 6 [L] bottle is used. 6 [L] bottles are often of steel, and as composite bottle is introduced, the volume is 
increased; hence the working time is increased as well [42]. 
 
 
==PAGE:67==
 
 
 
Johanne Salten – S170331 
 
Page 57 of 82 
Table 4-2: Air use pr. minute. 
Activity  
Liter air pr. minute [L/min] 
Rest 
10 
Light work 
30 
Heavy work 
60 
  
Available work time pr. smoke-helmet team is assumed to be 1500 [s] based on calculated in Table 4-3. 
Table 4-3: Available work time pr. smoke-helmet team. 
Available work time calculation  
At 50 bar a signal for pullback will sound. 
Search and rescue are assumed to heavy work. 
Pressure pr. bottle:   
300 [bar] – 50 [bar] = 250 [bar] 
Air pr. bottle: 
250 [bar] x 6 [L] = 1500 [L] 
Amount of available work time pr. smoke-helmet team: 
1500 [L] / 60 [L/min] = 25 [min] = 1500 [s] 
 
It is chosen to acknowledge the available work time of 1500 [s] per smoke-helmet team in the calculation. 1500 
[s] is considered as a resendable value. The time for search and rescue is given as total time for an area. To give 
a hint of sufficient resources, it is chosen to divide total time for search and rescues with available work time pr. 
smoke-helmet team, the result is an estimation of how many sessions it will require to complete the task. If more 
than one smoke-helmet team is available and simultaneously work is possible, is the total time for search and 
rescue divided by 1.5 (effect factor). For every additional available smoke-helmet team who work 
simultaneously, 0.5 is added to the effect factor. 
The effect factor is assumed by the author. It is chosen to divide by 1.5 + 0.5, instead of 2.0+1.0 as when smoke-
helmet teams work work-areas is most likely to overlap, and teams are most likely not put into action at the 
exact same time. Also, interchange of information between teams during a session takes time as well as replacing 
teams. Fatigue of the smoke-helmet team over time should be accounted for. No relevant data have been 
available on this matter, and therefore it is not included in this Master’s thesis. 
 
4.5.3 Fatigue of Firefighters During Intervention When Using Stairs 
When calculating the time for intervention, fatigue of firefighters should be accounted for. To which extent 
should depend on the purpose of the calculation. If the purpose of the calculation is determining the time for 
the initial intervention stages, fatigue is most likely not an issue. If the purpose is to determine the time and 
needed resources for both initial and final intervention, fatigue of firefighters is of interest as it influences the 
number of needed resources and a total of intervention. 
 
Regarding the present calculation, fatigue is relevant to some extent. Fatigue is relevant in the case where stairs 
only must be used. Time spent on ascending stairs with BA and equipment including necessary rest breaks 
prolongs the total time of intervention. It also has an impact on time spent on search and rescue, as firefighters 
use their energy on ascending which leaves less to conduct search and rescue. In relation to the use of stairs 
when a firefighter’s lift is available, FBIM data is used. The usage of stairs when the lift is used is limited, the 
result of accounting for fatigued will have very little effect and therefore can be ignored during initial 
intervention. Travel times where fatigue is accounted for will be based on data within FBIM and validated against 
data in a Master’s thesis by Rasmus Klüver Jensen [43] and in the research report “Assessment and Validation of 
the Fire Brigade Intervention Model for use within New Zealand and Performance-Based Fire Engineering” [25]. 
 
In the Master’s thesis by Rasmus Klüver Jensen “Redningsberedskabets intervention og indflydelse på evakuering 
i ultrahøje bygninger” [43] travel times for ascending stairs with regard to fatigue are addressed. 
Table 2-15 in the Master’s thesis list several tests in order to determine time travel times see Figure 4-4. 
==PAGE:68==
 
 
 
Johanne Salten – S170331 
 
Page 58 of 82 
 
Figure 4-4: Table 2-15 from master thesis by Rasmus Klüver Jensen [43]. Pause = break, RU = BA, MS = equipment, etage = Floor. 
Table is by courtesy of Rasmus Klüver Jensen. 
Values in table 2-15 are all converted at times from different test have different units and geometry of the stair 
is not given in all cases. The report has assumed a stair geometry. The Master’s thesis concludes time to ascend 
stairs including rest breaks is 0.48 [m/s], which corresponds to 1 [step/s]24. The conversion factor is 0.444 
[m/step] as given in [43]. It is assumed that the conversion factor can be used in this Master’s thesis as it is with 
regard to a standard Danish stair geometry which complies with Building Code 18 (BR18) §57 [22]. This would 
also be the case for the building used in the calculations. 
 
FBIM values for ascending stairs with BA and equipment is 0.9 [steps/s] and rest breaks is 1.9 [steps/s] [17]. If 
time for rest breaks is factored in time to ascend is 0.61 [steps/s]25. If converted time to ascend is 0.27 [m/s]26. 
 
FBIM values for time to ascend stairs where rest breaks are accounted for are conservative and is therefore used 
in this Master’s thesis. FBIM values for time to ascend stairs only are not conservative in relation to high-rise 
buildings. These values are only used on short distances in the calculations. FBIM values are  [25] compared to a 
test conducted in New Zealand (NZ). Table 33 in  [25] states the FBIM result inclusive of a rest break is 
conservative compared with NZ test results. But table 30 in  [25] states the time to ascend without rest breaks is 
not conservative when used above the 10th floor. 
                                                                
 
24 0,48 [m/s] x 0,444 [m/step] = 1,08 [step/s] 
25 100 [steps] / ((100 [steps] / 0.9 [steps/s]) + (100 [steps] / 1.9 [steps/s])) = 0.61 [steps/s] 
26 0,61 [steps/s] x 0.444 [m/step] = 0.27 [m/s] 
==PAGE:69==
 
 
 
Johanne Salten – S170331 
 
Page 59 of 82 
Time to pass landing is included in time pr. step in the above-mentioned results and data. But if the staircase is 
wide and therefore the landing distance is long, time to pass should be accounted for as it influences total time 
to ascend. If it is necessary to take more than one step on the landing before reaching the nest stair, the walk-
flow will change, and it is not reasonable to ignore the impact it will have on the total time to ascend. Attention 
should be paid to the landing distance above 1 meter between mid-stair and mid-stair. Time to pass landings is 
accounted for in the calculations. 
 
4.5.4 Safety Factors  
Fire safety engineering and performance-based design is a complex discipline. It is neither exact nor complete. 
The Realistic result is to be achieved, for this to happen informed approximations and expert judgment must be 
introduced. In order to ensure safety, appropriate margins are required in the FBIM analysis as it is in fire model 
and evacuation analysis. As FBIM is a way of determining the time for a decisions-based procedure, human 
interaction also adds to the complexity of the analysis. FBIM is a crude approximation of that which will occur in 
a real fire. It is, therefore, necessary suitable safety-margins or safety-factors is incorporated into any FBIM 
analysis when used in building design process, as it is in fire model and evacuation analysis. If no precautions are 
taken and FBIM results without safety-margins are compared with fire model and evacuations results, and the 
fire safety level is based hereupon, the fire safety level will not be a conservative estimation. Hence, the result 
will not be in favor of the safety of the FRS nor the safety of the occupants [17]. 
 
The FBIM manual [17] does not provide data for proper safety-margins, but it provides a method of applying 
these. The safety factor should be based upon an agreement with the local FRS funded in statically data for the 
local FRS and local intervention procedures. It is relevant that the safety margins are based on local FRS data due 
to variations of the Danish FRS, see section 2.1. 
 
How to apply safety factors are of interest as they affect the result. Safety factors should be applied step by step 
throughout the calculation. Times result of each chart depends either on statistically data or single values. When 
it is statistical data, a safety margin should be based on a percentile approach; mean values should be fair to use. 
When it is single value, data multiplying results with a factor should be sufficient. It is important that safety 
factors or margins are applied step by step as they could vary throughout the calculation due to different data is 
used and different data. Below is an example of the consequences of the different approaches [17]. 
 
The less data available, the more conservative should the safety factor or margin be. 
Example: 
 
Step by step approach  
 
 
Time to arrive                        t = 600 [s] 
Applying safety-factor, 1,5   t = 900 [s] 
Time to asses fire                   t = 200 [s] 
Applying safety-factor, 1,5   t = 300 [s] 
 
Flashover occur at t = 1000 [s], total time is 1200 [s] and 
fire cannot be extinguished before flashover this inflict 
on time to extinguish fire.  
 
Time to extinguish fire          t = 1800 [s] 
 
Total intervention time        t = 3000 [s] 
End-result approached   
Time to arrive   
t = 600 [s] 
Time to asses fire  
t = 200 [s] 
Flashover occur at t = 1000 [s], fire can be 
extinguished before flashover  
Time to extinguish fire 
t = 900 [s] 
 
Total intervention time               t = 1700 [s] 
Applying safety-factor, 1,5         t = 2550 [s] 
 
 
The different approaches have an impact on the estimated time of suppression, which is not in favor of safety in 
general. In this Master’s thesis, it is chosen not to account for safety factors. The result not compared with a fire 
model or evacuation analysis. The calculations are compared against each other to identify the effect of different 
interventions procedures; therefore, safety factors and margins can be ignored.

5 Results and Discussion   
This section displays the results and a discussion of these. It is chosen to keep it as two separate sections as a 
result is both shown in the table and diagrams. The discussion is divided into section as this analysis consists of 
different separate elements which when put together form the total analysis. 
5.1 Results 
The result from the different calculations is shown below. Table 5-1 displays the different combinations of 
calculations, of full-time and part-time firefighters, if SOP, stairs or lift is used.  
Figure 5-1, Figure 5-2, Figure 5-3 and Figure 5-4 display results of calculations 1.x to 4.x. Figure 5-5 displays results 
of calculation 5.1 and 6.1. 
 
Below, the general data conclusions are listed. As the calculations have the same starting point and are based 
on the same assumptions, the number of firefighters only influence the number of activities which can be 
performed concurrently which not necessarily have an effect on time to reach fire compartment, etc. 
 
Part-time firefighters are 300 [s] slower than full-time firefighters when the time to reach fire 
compartment is compared. This is due to the time difference in chart 3. Time to reach the kerbside is 
600 [s] if full-time and 900 [s] if part-time. This applies to all Danish calculations. 
 
Time to reach the fire compartment is increased by a factor of 3.4 if full-time firefighters use the stairs 
instead of the lift. The factor is 3 if part-time firefighters. In both cases, no SOP is used and assumed 
standard intervention procedure is used. This applies to calculation 1.x - HL+3, calculation 2.x - HL+5 and 
calculation 3.x - HL+7. 
 
Time for additional resources to arrive is increased by a factor of ≈ 2.0 if stairs are used. If additional 
arriving resources are part-time firefighters, time of arrival is increased by 300 [s], as dispatch time is 
longer if part-time firefighters compared to full-time firefighters. In this case, no SOP is used and 
assumed standard intervention procedure is used. This applies to calculation 1.x - HL+3, calculation 2.x 
- HL+5 and calculation 3.x - HL+7. 
Other data conclusions are listed below relevant figures.

5.2 Discussion 
The results show a change in intervention times in relation to if a SOP is used or not and the number of firefighters 
available. 
 
Calculation 1.x though 4.x are a reflection of the collected data in section 2.2.2 regarding Danish intervention 
methods and different combination of employment. Data within FBIM do not, in general, distinguish between 
whether it is part- or full-time firefighters. The only consideration made in FBIM is in chart 3, where it is possible 
to distinguish between if the station is manned full-time or not. It is assumed that data within FBIM is a result of 
a wide spectrum of firefighters and is therefore valid for both full- and part-time FRS. 
 
Regarding the second deployment, it is chosen to only state when it will arrive and not what it consists of and 
which tasks, it will be assigned to. What the second deployment consists of is entirely based on the ISL/OIC 
initial reconnaissance and what the ISL/OIC finds necessary. This will vary from ISL/OIC to ISL/OIC. The second 
deployment is not relevant when only looking at the initial intervention stages, but it could be relevant in 
relation to the success rate of the final intervention. 
 
Figure 5-6: Comparison of results. The first calculation of all Danish calculations is compared to international internal intervention procedures. 
Calculation 1.1, 2.1, 3.1, 4.1, 5.1 and 6.1 is displayed.

Figure 5-6 show international and Danish results when the lift is used. A difference in when firefighters first 
arrived at the fire compartment ready to conduct search and rescue is to be seen. The difference is small when 
comparing Sweden, FDNY, and HL+7 using lift SOP. The three strategies are also alike, and a SOP is put into action 
in all cases. The one thing they have in common is defining the OIC/ISL does not check which detector is activated 
or the location of the fire. This is either laid out to HL/captain or a team of firefighters as a standard task. The 
difference between the intervention procedure described in the Danish lift SOP and the international SOPs, is in 
the Danish SOP HL follows ISL to the ABA control panel in order to collect information regarding activated 
detector, and afterwards HL and firefighters move toward the fire compart. In the Swedish and FDNY SOP, a team 
of firefighters move directly toward the building and the fire compartment, information regarding where to 
locate the activated detector is give on the go. The difference between calculation 4.1 and 5.1 - 6.1 are 
respectively 118.6 [s] and 176.1 [s]. But if calculation 4.1, 5.1 and 6.1 are compared with calculation 1.1, 2.1 and 
3.1 a difference of respectively 307.2 [s] – 346.7 [s] is found. So, an effect is to be found in intervention times if 
a SOP is put into action. The difference is small, about than 5 minutes, but 5 minutes can make a difference when 
dealing with fires. Comparing the results of this size should be donned with caution as to many sources of errors 
exits within FBIM, the number of assumptions made in order to complete the calculation and when FBIM is 
applied in a different context. 
 
There is no difference in the critical path between calculation 1.1, 2.1 and 3.1 in this analysis. The difference 
between these three calculations is the time for setting up water supply, which is carried out more efficiently in 
relation to the number of firefighters increase, but it has no influence on the critical path as the time to assess 
the fire on the 30th floor takes longer. But if the fire was on a lower floor, time to set up water supply would be 
the definite time in relation to calculation 1.x - HL+3, as additional resources do not arrive until after firefighters 
have reached the fire compartment in general. A permanent water supply must be set-up before entering the 
building as a pump appliance water tank often is small and will be insufficient in short time. It will most likely 
only be enough to fill a hose and dry riser system. The difference or lack of between calculations 1.x, 2.x and 3.x 
could be due to lack of obtained information compared to calculations where a SOP is used. Many assumptions 
haven been made which could be a source of error in the calculations. The number of necessary assumptions 
which is needed to make in order to complete the calculations could have been different, if it had been chosen 
to focus on just one Danish FRS. In this case a more detailed investigation could have been conducted, but the 
result would have been with regard to that particular station and not a general perspective. It was chosen not to 
focus on one particular Danish FRS as it was of interest to investigate if FBIM in general could be applied. Also, 
the applied intervention method is a texts book solution, which is a very conservative standard intervention 
strategy which is not develop with a focus on high-rises. It is most likely ISL will not include a walk to the fire 
compartment in the initial reconnaissance, it would be a reasonable decision. So, if it is assumed ISL will not walk 
to the fire compartment a difference is to be found. In this case, HL+5 (calculation 2.1) and HL+7 (calculation 3.1) 
are uniform and time for firefighters to reach the fire compartment will be 972.2 [s]. Whereas HL+3 (calculation 
1.1) are the slowest with 1193,4 [s], which is 221,2 [s] in difference compared with HL+5 and HL+7. This applies 
in any case regarding part-time or full-time, but if stairs are used no difference between the three crew sizes is 
to be found due to the prolonged access time. Time to reach the fire compartment is in this case 2054,0 [s].  
 
In general, if stairs are used, time for firefighters to reach the fire compartment is increased by a factor of 
respectively 3.4 if full-time firefighters and 3.0 if part-time firefighters in any case where a SOP is not used. If a 
SOP is put into action the time is increased be a factor of 2 in any case. Therefore, it has a positive effect when 
the firefighter lift is used. 
 
Time for additional resources to arrive is almost similar in all the different calculations. The time given in FDNY is 
fictive as all resources are dispatched at the same time, but most likely not from the same station. Time for 
additional resources to arrive is increased when only part-time firefighters are present. This is due to longer 
dispatch time. 
 
==PAGE:79==
 
 
 
Johanne Salten – S170331

Total Intervention Time Compared with Fire Rating of Constructions  
If the total intervention time is compared with the fire rating of the loadbearing construction a possible conflict 
is identified, see Figure 5-7. The loadbearing constructions is design with a 2-hour (7200 [s]) fire rating.  
 
 
Figure 5-7: Comparison of results. The first calculation of all Danish calculations is compared to international internal intervention procedures. 
Calculation 1.1, 2.1, 3.1, 4.1, 5.1 and 6.1 is displayed. Time given in seconds in Figure 5-6 are converted into clock-hours.  
The total intervention time, which include a fictive time of search and rescue is between one hour and one and 
a half hour when a firefighter lift is used. The calculated times does not include time to rescue a person from the 
fire compartment, it does not include fatigue of firefighters, it does not include time for replacement of smoke-
helmet teams, the time does not include time for suppression and it is calculations with no regard to safety 
factors. A lot of time contributing  activities are not addressed in the calculations, but if safety factors, time for 
suppression, etc. would have been accounted for time of intervention would most likely exceed time of fire rating 
which can be an issue. If the intervention is carried out by part-time firefighters the total time of intervention is 
increased, which is to the disadvantage of the situation. This is only a very sketchy study as it was not a focus 
point of this Master’s thesis.

Time of Response and the Impact of This 
The time of response chosen to use in the calculations is static based on the previously common practice used in 
Denmark. The time does not favor a building if it is located close to a fire station. Fire stations can be moved over 
time, and this must not impact the fire safety level of the building. Therefore, a static time based on the agreed 
service level within the coverage area should be used for calculation purposes. This will be a conservative 
assumption. In FBIM it is possible to account for the exact location of the fire station.  
It is chosen to use the same dispatch time for secondary arriving units. This is not a conservative assumption, as 
this fire station most likely is located further away from the building. But as time is only used to give a rough 
estimation on when additional resources possibly will arrive compared to the state of the intervention, it seems 
reasonable to use the same time. Also, the purpose of the calculation is not to determine if the building can be 
located at a certain location in Denmark, but if FBIM can be used to analyze initial intervention. Second-arriving 
units are not part of the initial intervention. 
 
Importance of Firefighter Employment 
It is chosen to analyze interventions with both full-time, part-time and a combination hereof deployed resources 
as this is of importance in relation to when first-deployed resources arrive and when second-deployed resources 
arrive. The importance of when first-deployed resources arrive is important compared to the development of 
the fire and evacuation. It is relevant if the FRS arrive before or after flashover, as this determines the success 
rate of suppression. Also, if evacuation is not completed upon arrival, this can demand attention from the FRS. 
Both the SOP from Sweden and FDNY allocates resources to deal with occupants, evacuation and search and 
rescue of adjacent enclosures. Evacuation is not dealt with in Denmark as standard. If evacuation demands 
attention from FRS as no resources are pre-assigned to this, it will impact available resources to conduct 
suppression and search and rescue of the fire compartment. 
 
Fatigue of Firefighters and Search and Rescue Time 
It has been chosen to account for the fatigue of firefighters when using stairs. This is primarily due to the 
availability of data. Fatigue of firefighters are not only important in relation to the use of stairs, but it is important 
throughout the entire intervention. Time for search and rescue is based on FBIM data, perimeter length, and 
area of the fire compartment. It is not possible for one smoke-helmet team to complete the search within the 
available work time. Therefore,  the calculation time for search and rescue is fictive. But it gives a rough indication 
of the time that is going to be spent on the task alone with no regard to replacement of teams and fatigue. The 
time for search and rescue is divided by 1.5 if two smoke-helmet teams are available to work concurrently. This 
is also to give a rough indication of the benefits of having multiple teams working at the same time. 
In relation to the calculated time for search and rescue, fatigue of firefighters should be accounted for as it will 
prolong the intervention over time. Also, the BA limit must be considered. It is not acceptable to assume 
firefighters can conduct sessions of search and rescue endlessly. Trade guideline state firefighters are only to use 
up two bottles of compressed air per day during training exercises, and a 30-minute work break between the 
two sessions are needed [11]. This also applies to some extent to real fires. Therefore, the calculated time for 
search and rescue is fictive, but it can contribute to an estimation of time spent on this activity, the possible 
needed resources to resolve the activity and benefits of having multiple available teams. 
 
Safety Precautions 
In relation to the calculations and definition of the different intervention procedures, no consideration to safety 
precaution has been giving in relation to firefighter safety during smoke diving. This should be considered when 
determining the needed resources to be deployed to a certain building type. Especially in relation to fires in high-
rises as these are complex due to the height of the building and the logistics of equipment and relief teams. 
DEMA and the Danish Working Environment Authority wrote a common pronouncement in 2004 [44] stating 
that a safety team should be ready when a smoke-helmet team conducts smoke diving in risk areas if the session 
exposes a risk to the team. The need for a safety team is based on the risk judgment conducted by the ISL/OIC. 
If a hazardous incident occurs in the fire compartment and the safety team is located on the ground floor, the 
==PAGE:81==
 
 
 
Johanne Salten – S170331 
 
Page 71 of 82 
time to ascend is possibly too long to make a difference, especially if the stairs is to be used. This matter is only 
addressed in the FDNY SOP [14]. Other SOPs address the issue of relief teams, but not the safety team. In relation 
to calculation time for search and rescue no consideration is given to a safety team. If this were to be done, time 
for search and rescue should not be divided by 1.5. 
 
Calculated Times in Relation to Other Studies  
The calculated times using FBIM is fictive but based on life-like data. Therefore, the difference between Sweden, 
FDNY and HL+7 SOP is to be taken with a grain of salt. FBIM with the current level of data basis is not fit to analyze 
time differences on a small scale with great accuracy, especially not when no safety factors are used.  
But it is fair to use FBIM to identify if a change in steps can have an effect in strategies.  
Time results found in this analysis compared to previous Danish studies conducted by Lise Kamstrup and Therese 
Franck in 2012 [1] do not match. The test is performed in a similar building compared to the high-rise used in this 
Master’s thesis, but the fire is located on the 24th floor instead of the 30th floor. The full-scale test does not 
account for the time of response and time is started when the FRS arrive at the kerbside. The assumed 
intervention procedure as described in section 2.2.2.4 is comparable with the described procedure used in the 
full-scale test. Time for firefighters to reach fire compartment on the 24th floor is in the full-scale test 774 [s] 
when the time to locate firefighters lift is left out, the lift is in the full-scale test located behind a door. The 
calculated time in FBIM for HL+7 SOP is 355 [s], which is less compared to the full-scale test even when the 
difference in floors are accounted for.  
If the individual time steps are compared, the calculated FBIM results are less conservative compared to the full-
scale test. As an example, time for gathering information in chart 5 is 2 [min] and 13 [s], the average time in the 
full-scale test is for intervention on the 24th floor is 3 [min] and 57 [s]. The time determined in the full-scale test 
is found to be reasonable when considering the Danish set procedure. This can vary from set procedures in 
Australia, and as a data basis for FBIM have not been available it is not possible to determine the cause.  
In 2014, Jan Riis, Peter Dræbye and Rasmus Storegaard conducted an analysis of a series of standard intervention  
exercises displayed in the Master’s thesis “Design of Interventions Capacity for the Fire Brigade – Analysis of 
Reaction Times” [4]. Exercises 2b HL+5 is a “stigrørsudlægning” to the 2nd floor. The standard intervention 
procedure is the intervention procedure described in section 2.2 of this Master’s thesis. The analysis distinguishes 
between the employment of the firefighters, but, the overall average time of completion of exercise 2b is 157,3 
[s]. If this is compared to results from FBIM in an adjusted HL+5 calculation, a similarity is found. From FBIM time 
for gathering tools (chart 6), time taken to travel to set up area (chart 8) and time taken to set up water for initial 
firefighter protection (chart 10) are relevant. The sum of these three elements is when adjusted from 30 floors 
to two, 181 [s], which is a 15 % deviation from the exercise result. This deviation is reasonable considering the 
circumstances and sample size. 
The former studies suggest FBIM can be used but with circumspection. The method features the relevant 
element, but time results must be evaluated in every case as well as calculation procedure. The above deviation 
in time results and the number of necessary calculation assumptions also clearly indicate why input and output 
of the FBIM must be reviewed by the relevant FRS, as stated in section 3.2.1 in relation to using FBIM in the fire 
safety design process. 
 
The result is what was expected when textbook solutions are compared with strategies developed based on 
experience. The textbook solutions were never intended to be used in a high-rise building as these are developed 
in other building-contexts. The result is not a clear statement of which method is the best, but they are a 
statement that FBIM can identify the effects of different methods and can be used in a Danish context with 
caution. If FBIM were to be used in the long run in Denmark the flowchart should be adjusted as there is some 
variation between Denmark and Australia. Chart 2, 3, and 4 should be adjusted to match time of response and 
procedure for this. Chart 7 should be extended to include modern intervention strategies. Chart 10 and 11 should 
be adjust as set up of water supply is traditionally done otherwise in Denmark as in Australia. The overall 
structure of FBIM is applicable. 
 
 
==PAGE:82==
 
 
 
Johanne Salten – S170331 
 
Page 72 of 82 
6 Conclusion 
The FBIM results have demonstrated, FBIM can identify different effects of different intervention strategies and 
procedures. The total time of intervention is increased when no SOP is used when dealing with a fire in a high-
rise. Time differences are identified between both different Danish intervention strategies as well as 
international intervention strategies. The Swedish method is most likely comparable with the Danish FRS 
traditions. The Swedish method is more efficient than the identified Danish SOP for when using firefighter lifts. 
The difference lies in if firefighters can proceed straight to the building and get information on the way to the 
relevant fire compartment, or if they must wait on the HL/Captain before entering the building. It is a difference 
in the division of responsibilities during the intervention which has an effect. 
When stairs are used, time taken to reach the fire compartment and time for being ready to conduct search and 
rescue is increased by a factor of 3.0 - 3.4. When stairs are used, fatigue of firefighters becomes relevant as the 
firefighter’s energy is spent on traveling instead of search and rescue and later on suppression. This influences 
the time for when replacement of the firefighters is needed during the intervention and time of completing the 
replacement. 
FBIM as an overall methodology can be used in Denmark and is a rational way of documenting means of 
intervention according to BR18 and performance-based design in general. FBIM can be used to quantify the 
individual components that make up the overall FRS dispatch and intervention time in relation to performance-
based building designs. The flowcharts should, over time, be adjusted to Danish intervention traditions in terms 
of choice of words, typical reconnaissance procedures and water supply choices. Data with FBIM should also, 
over time, be adjusted to Danish standard interventions times as more detailed time steps are preferable. It is of 
importance that FBIM is not used as a standalone model and interaction with the relevant FRS must be secured 
when conducting a calculation. FBIM can be used as a tool for analyzing interventions and on the basis of this 
state the safety precautions in relation to necessary resources at the scene in order to perform a safe and 
effective intervention. The methodology behind FBIM could contribute to a better common understanding 
between the FRS and fire safety consultants of the building design and the fire safety level of this.

Nomenclature 
Glossary and Technical Term 
 
Automatic fire alarm 
system 
ABA 
An automatic fire alarm system. It is a system consisting of smoke detectors, 
thermal detectors, etc. These are connected to a central system which upon 
activation sends a direct uninterrupted signal to local fire station.  
The system is the central control system, upon activation it can also activate other 
fire safety systems such as warning systems, fire door closing systems, it can stop 
ventilations system (HVAC), etc. 
System can also be activated by call point (brandtryk). 
Automatic warning 
system 
AVA 
Automatic warning system. 
The system is either activated by the ABA system or a call point. 
The system can either be a tone warning system or a speech warning system, 
depending in which building the system is installed. 
The goal for the system is to initiate evacuation of a building early in the fire 
developing process. 
AFAC 
Abbreviation: AFAC 
AFAC is the Australian and New Zealand National Council for fire, emergency 
services and land management, creating synergies across the emergency 
management sector [45]. 
www.afac.com.au  
Appliance 
The word used to describe all types of fire vehicles. Other use terms such as engines, 
tenders, turntable ladders, small vehicles, etc. 
 
Pump appliance = Automobilsprøjte/spøjte 
Ladder appliance = Stigevoge/drejestige/lift 
Water appliance = Vandtakvogn  
ASET 
Available safe egress time.  
The time calculated e.g. via CFD calculation, two-zone calculations or basic 
calculations for when tenability criteria are met in relation to occupant safety. Time 
is measured from time of ignition. It can be the time for when the smoke layer is in 
a specific height, or the temperature in a room exceed a specific limit, etc. 
Danish term “tiden til kritiske forhold”. 
Branch 
Also called nozzle. Danish term is “strålerør”. 
Breathing apparatus 
Abbreviation: BA 
The BA is a device consisting of a full face mask with a nose cup, tubes, back plate, 
etc. The device provides breathable air for the firefighter. 
Bunker gear 
The suit the firefighters ware this include helmet, gloves, boots, undergarment. 
Captain 
See Danish term “Holdleder”. 
Company 
A ladder or engine COMPANY is the relevant appliance and the person in/on it. 
==PAGE:87==
 
 
 
Johanne Salten – S170331 
 
Page 77 of 82 
Computational Fluid 
Dynamics 
Abbreviation: CFD 
A CFD simulation of a fire is a computer-based calculation of a fire scenario. It 
determines the most likely fire development based on the data input, such as fire 
expected fire growth, the maximum size of the fire and the location of the fire and 
the surroundings. 
Commander-room  
Danish term “brandmandscentral”. A room equipped with the fire alarm system 
panel, drawings of the building, etc. All for the OIC to get a good overview of the 
building. 
Consultant 
The person / persons whose task is to prepare the fire technical documentation or 
other relevant documents in acc. with a specific building project. Danish term 
“Rådgiver”. 
DBI / DBI guideline  
DBI stands for Danish Fire and Safety Technology Institute, Danish term “Dansk 
Brand- og sikringsteknisk Institut”. 
The institute publish guidelines for design and installing fire proception system, and 
fire safety in general – these are DBI guidelines. 
The guidelines are widely used in Denmark.  
District plan 
A district plan is a document outlining the city council's plans for the management 
of a piece of land or area in its district or city. Danish Term “lokalplan”. 
Don 
“To don BA”, it means to put the BA on. Danish term “iføre sig”. 
Emergency dispatch 
center 
24/7/365 full-time staff call center for emergency calls only. This not only for fire 
incidents, but also medical or police matters. 
Danish term “alarmcentralen” or “1-1-2 centralen”. 
FBIM 
Fire Brigade Intervention Model. A computer program developed by AFAC in the 
90’ties. 
Final-intervention  
The activities being undertaken to complete the intervention [46]. (Den indsats, der 
iværksættes med henblik på at afslutte løsningen af beredskabets opgaver.)  
Danish-term “endelig indsats”. 
Fire extinguishing 
medias 
The proper media chosen for extinguishing in a certain fire incident. A media can be 
water, foam, CO2 and/or chemical powder etc. 
Fire compartment 
A fire compartment is the floor, room, area in which the fire has started. The 
compartment is defined by the primary fire compartment boundaries 
(brandsektion). 
Fire information panel 
Digital panel which presents information regarding detector activation, etc. Danish 
ter “ABA betjenings panel” or “brandmandspanel”. 
Fire model  
CFD-analysis or 2-zone calculation of the development of the fire. Also see 
“Computational Fluid Dynamics” and “ASET”. 
Fire scenario 
A well-defined design fire that is most likely to occur within the building. The 
scenario is based upon a risk screening of the building. 
==PAGE:88==
 
 
 
Johanne Salten – S170331 
 
Page 78 of 82 
Full time 
FRS/firefighter 
Persons who works as a firefighter on a full-time basis (paid). Firefighters work in a 
fire stations. Vehicles can resume immediately upon alerting. Danish term 
“fuldtidsbrandmand”. 
High-pressure hose 
Hose mounted on the appliance, it is ready for use with in short time. Danish term 
“højstryksslange”. 
Holdleder (HL) 
Holdleder is the Danish term. It is chosen to use this term though out the Master’s 
thesis in relation with Danish affairs. When talking about foreign affairs, other terms 
are used such as captain/officer/sub-officer. 
The person who safeguards the safety and technical leadership of a team of 
firefighters. The person is trained for this and is a trained firefighter before 
becoming the HL. 
HL + X 
The abbreviation describes a unit consisting of a HL and his/her crew 
members/firefighters. Normally, the numbers of firefighters will be 3, 5 or 7.  
 
HL+3 = one Holdleder + 3 firefighters  
Incident commander 
post 
Location where incident leaders and other participants such medical staff, the 
police, etc. meet to plan the salvage operation or coordination hereof. Danish term 
“kommunikationsstade (KST)”.  
Indsatsleder 
(ISL/OIC/IC) 
Indsatsleder is the Danish term. It is chosen to use this term throughout the 
Master’s thesis in relation with Danish affairs. When talking about foreign affairs, 
other terms are used such as Onsite Incident Commander (OIC). Theo OIC have the 
overall technical leadership of the incident. Other arriving incident commanders (IC) 
have the same education as OIC but will assume other posts. This will correspond 
to the Danish term “Skadestedsleder”.  
ISL - The person who has the overall technical leadership of an operation. He/she 
instructs the HL in which areas they oversee. They are a trained firefighter and HL 
before becoming an ISL. Other arriving ISL will assume position of sector chief 
(Skadestedleder). 
Initial intervention 
The preliminary activities carried out to provide first aid to injured/threatened 
persons and prevent the spread of damage. Once the initial intervention has been 
initiated, the ISL can conduct a more thorough reconnaissance of the entire scene 
with the intent to plan final-intervention [46]. 
(Den foreløbige indsats, der tilsigter at yde en første hjælp til tilskadekomne og 
umiddelbart truede personer samt at hindre udbredelse af en skade. Når 
førsteindsatsen er iværksat, har indsatsledelsen mulighed for at gennemføre en 
nøjere rekognoscering af hele skadestedet med henblik på iværksættelsen af den 
endelige indsats.) 
Danish term “første indsats”. 
Initial reconnaissance 
and/or 
Final reconnaissance 
Abbreviation: RECCE 
ISL (or whom is in charge) conduct initial reconnaissance in the beginning of an 
event/accident. Important information is gathered, the scene is inspected in 
relation to hazards and an overview of the situation is created. Based on this, the 
intervention method and plan for initial intervention is made. Danish term 
“situationsbedømmelse”. 
==PAGE:89==
 
 
 
Johanne Salten – S170331 
 
Page 79 of 82 
In FBIM, there is no distinguishing between RECCE as there is in Denmark as 
described in “Retningslinjer for indsatsledelse” [46]. Therefore, it chosen in this 
Master’s thesis to call it initial reconnaissance and final reconnaissance. 
Kerbside 
Location just outside the premises on which a building is on fire. 
Danish term “fortovskant”. 
Time to reach this location is important as some premises can be behind gates and 
fences which must be passed. 
Kitchenette 
A kitchenette is a small kitchen where it is possible to make a cup of tea/coffee. In 
modern office buildings, a kitchenette often has a large coffee maker/machine, 
electric kettle, microwave oven, a dishwasher, etc. It is not a cooking area with a 
stove or oven. 
Nozzel 
See branch 
Onsite Incident 
Commander 
Danish term “Indsatsleder”. 
Officer 
See Holdleder 
Operating organization 
See Danish term “driftsorganisation”. 
O-plans 
O-plans are drawings made especially for locating ABA-detectors. The drawings are 
located near the ABA-central panel. The ABA-panel informs which drawings are 
relevant in relation to which detector is activated. 
Part-time firefighter 
Persons who works as a firefighter on a part-time basis (paid). The employment is 
in addition to a full-time job in another company. Danish term “Deltidsbrandmand”. 
RSET 
Required safe egress time 
The time measured from fire ignition that is required for occupants to evacuate a 
building/area. Evacuation can be to the outside or to a safe location inside the 
building, from where occupants can reach the outside 
Picklist 
A picklist is a standard list of the first message to the fire and rescue service from 
the emergency dispatch center. It is a universal list and is used by all fire and rescue 
services in Denmark. 
Danish term “Pickliste”. 
Scene/Incident scene 
The site where an incident has occurred and where there is a need for intervention 
by emergency services. The damage area is bounded by the internal barrier [46]. 
Smoke-helmet fireman 
The firefighter who enters a building on fire and/or environment where normal 
breathing is not possible. The firefighter wears a breathing apparatus and bunker 
gear. Danish term “Røgdykker”. 
Is also called a BA-team.  
Suppression  
The sum of all the work done by firefighters to extinguish a fire from the time of its  
discovery until extinguished. Danish term “slukning”. 
Fire tower 
An enclosed stairway connected at each story by an outside balcony or fireproof 
vestibule vented to the outside. Danish term “sikkerhedstrappe”. 
==PAGE:90==
 
 
 
Johanne Salten – S170331 
 
Page 80 of 82 
SOP 
Standard operation procedure – a written manual of the intervention in a specific 
situation. Everybody within the fire and rescue service has specific task to complete 
and every task is codependent. An SOP can optimize the intervention and reduce 
time spent completing the overall task to extinguish fire in an apartment. 
It can also be called an action card. 
Tabletop scenario 
An exercise / learning method known from the technical leadership courses and 
other learning situations. The "game" is based on fictive incidents created in a 
miniature city. Danish term “planspil senarie”. 
Volunteer 
Persons who works as a firefighter for voluntary employment beside their full-time 
job. It is unpaid. Danish term “Frivillig brandmand”. 
§60-corporation 
A §60-corporation is company established in accordance with §60 in the law 
regarding municipality (kommunestyrelsesloven). It is a number of former 
individual municipal fire brigades that team up and form one fire brigade. 
 
 
==PAGE:91==
 
 
 
Johanne Salten – S170331

Exercise Diagrams 
Below are diagrams of the different exercises shown. The diagrams are from “Assessment and Validation of the Fire 
Brigade Intervention Model for use within New Zealandand Performance-Based Fire Engineering” [25] and by courtesy 
of Principal Fire Engineer Ed Claridge, Auckland Council New Zealand. 
 
Exercise 1 – Hose drill 
 
 
Exercise 2 - Hydrant riser operations (booster system) 
 
 
Exercise 3 – taking hose up stirs  
Drill 1  
 
 
Connect and charge sufficient lengths of 
feeder hose between a pump appliance 
and the fire hydrant. 
Drill 2 
2 firefighters, dismount with BA (or 
remove from locker and don). Lay and 
charge not less than two 25 m lengths of 
delivery hose between pump appliance 
and branch. 
 
Drill 1  
Connect and charge sufficient lengths of 
hose between a pump appliance and the 
fire hydrant a. Lay and charge sufficient 
lengths of delivery hose between pump 
appliance and hydrant riser/booster inlets. 
Drill 2  
 
 
Firefighters don BA lay out hose and 
connect to booster outlet, charge system 
and flow attack line.  
 
==PAGE:111==
 
 
 
Johanne Salten – S170331 
 
Page XIX of XXIX 
 
 
Exercise 5 – Search and rescue  
 
  
 
 
 
 
Drill 1  
2 firefighters to ascend stairs with hose. 
When head of stairs is reached show 
water. Water to be taken from the 
appliance only. 
 
Drill 2 
Connect and charge sufficient lengths of 
feeder hose between a pump appliance 
and the fire hydrant. 
 
Drill 1  
Conduct a search appropriate to the size of 
the room/space as per usual procedures. 
 
Drill 2 
Position an adult size dummy (victim) to be 
rescued remote from the enclosure exit 
point. Send in a different search team to 
that which completed drill 1 and record 
area searched until victim located  
 
Drill 3 
Record the time taken for 2 firefighters, 
wearing BA with obscured face masks, to 
carry a high-rise pack or similar to ascend, 
and descend a recorded number of steps or 
treads (risers). A typical case would be 3 
flights of stairs.

charge hose.
